Further Materialist Views: Four Natures and Single Nature
Maturidi presents two further materialist views and critiques the argument for a Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional materialist positions: one that makes the four natures the origin but with substances having their own origin, and another that posits a single nature as the cause of the world, leading to a flawed argument for a Maker.

Core Idea
Materialists who affirm a Maker still end up with contradictions.

After presenting the first materialist group, Imam al-Maturidi describes two more nuanced positions. One group views the four natures as the origin but considers natures incidental to substances. Another group posits a single nature that necessitates the world's eternity, yet they attempt to prove the existence of a Maker through the coherence of creation. Maturidi points out the inconsistency in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sub-passage covering both groups. Note the transition from 'وَمِنْهُمْ' (and among them).
2
Identify the two groups
The first subgroup says the origin is the four natures but each substance has its own origin. The second says there is one nature that causes the world.
3
Understand the argument for a Maker
The second group uses the coherence and perfection of things to prove a knowing Manager, because nature lacks power.
They affirm a Maker.
4
Spot the flaw
They then claim the world is eternal, which contradicts the idea of a powerful Creator whose power would necessitate creation. The author ends with 'There is no power except with God.'
This is the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنهم اختلفوا : فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا ، من غير أن يكون له صنع وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع : إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاصيلها ، وسماها قوم هيولى ، والتفاوت فى الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ : إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله ، وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع ، والدواب من اضطرابه ، وعلى هذا كل شيء . ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع ، ولكن لكل جوهر أصلا ، والطبائع دخيلة فيها. ومنهم من جعله كذلك بالطبائع ويقول : هو واحد ، ويجعله علة تكون العالم ، فيوجب قدمه بوجوده ، ويذهب في اثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء واتقانها ؛ إذ ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدير عليم ، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة ، و به صلاح الأشياء ، فقالوا بالصانع ، ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم فى الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعلاها ، على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه و تعميه و تبعمه ، وأنه قادر بذاته فثبت وجوده وكرمه بذاته ، فيلزم كون الذي كرمه يوجبه ، وقدرته توجده ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
But they differed: some of them made it as we described, without any act of creation, and upon this goes the school of the adherents of natures (taba'i'): that difference and diversity are according to the diversity of natures and their details, and some people called them matter (hayula). The diversity in what I mentioned is like the example of dyes: they come out in different colors due to the difference and balance of the mixture. Upon that, they considered the substance of humans from the balance of natures, and of beasts from their imbalance, and upon this everything. And among them is one who thinks its origin is the four natures, but each substance has an origin, and the natures are incidental in them. And among them is one who made it likewise by natures and says: it is one, and he makes it the cause of the world's coming-to-be, thereby necessitating its eternity by its existence. He goes in proving the Maker to the coherence and perfection of things, for that cannot be except by a knowing Manager, for nature does not refer to power, and through Him is the good order of things. So they affirmed the Maker. Then when He was eternal, they necessitated the world's coming-to-be in eternity in the manner of the connection of things with their causes, upon that when the world was [??], and that He is powerful by His essence, so His existence and generosity are established by His essence. Therefore, it follows that what He has bestowed necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage classifies and describes various materialist views about the origin of the world. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins by noting that those who reject divine action in the world still differ among themselves. The first group described are those who attribute all diversity to the four natures (taba'i') and their combinations, comparing them to dyes that produce different colors based on mixture. They believe that human essence is from a balance of natures, while animals are from imbalance. Then he mentions a subgroup that considers the four natures as origins but each substance has its own origin with natures being incidental. Another group posits a single nature as the cause of the world, necessitating the world's eternity, and then tries to prove the Maker through the coherence and perfection of things, arguing that nature does not have power. They end up affirming a Maker but then claim the world is eternal, which leads to a contradiction: if the Maker is powerful by essence, then His power would necessitate the world's existence eternally. The author concludes this section with the statement 'There is no power except with God', indicating the insufficiency of such views.

Key Terms
طبائع (taba'i')
أربع (four)
صانع (Maker)
قدرة (power)
أزل (eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all materialists deny a Creator completely.
Correction: The second subgroup actually affirms a Maker, but their argument leads to an eternal world, which is problematic.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason the second subgroup gives for proving a Maker?
A) The existence of evil
B) The coherence and perfection of things
C) The diversity of natures
D) The need for a first cause

Key Takeaway
The materialists' attempt to combine a single nature with a Maker leads to the problematic conclusion that the world is eternal alongside God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the intricate order of the universe, does it ever feel easier to believe it always existed rather than submit to a Creator who brought it into being from nothing? What part of you resists that submission?
?
The materialists in this lesson used the same evidence of design that you might use to argue for God, yet they ended up with a world that is eternal and independent. How can you ensure that your reasoning about God does not lead you to compromise the truth of His absolute power and freedom?
?
If you truly believed that God's power is not bound by any necessity—not even the necessity of an eternal universe—how would that change the way you think about your own life, your struggles, and the times when you feel trapped by circumstances? What would it look like to live in that freedom today?

The Theory of an Original Element (Clay)
Maturidi presents the view of an original element (clay).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that the world originated from a single primordial element, specifically clay, and how it differs from Islamic monotheism.

Core Idea
The claim that the world came from clay reduces the Creator to a craftsman.

This lesson focuses on one of the ancient theories about the world's origin: that it was fashioned from a pre-existing substance, like clay. Maturidi presents this view to show its flaws and contrast it with Islamic belief in creation ex nihilo. Understanding this helps clarify why theologians rejected materialist origins.

Guided Path
1
Locate the theory in the text
Read the first part of the passage: 'ومنهم من يقول ... أحدث البارى منها هذا العالم'. Identify the phrase that states the original element (طينة).
The word طينة (tina) means 'clay'.
2
Understand the claim
This view posits that the world was crafted from a pre-existing origin, specifically clay. The Creator is described as 'originating' (أحدث) the world from this clay.
3
Compare with Islamic doctrine
Consider how this differs from the Islamic belief that Allah created everything from nothing. If the world is made from pre-existing matter, the Creator is not the ultimate originator.
4
Notice the language
Observe that the term 'أصل' (origin) and 'صنعة' (craft) imply a kind of making that relies on a substrate, which is incompatible with absolute creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول : هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه ، اختلفوا : فمنهم من يجعل أصلة طينة ، أحدث البارى منها هذا العالم . وقوم جعلوا النجوم والشمس بما كن يجرين دائبات ، ويجريان نشوء العالم . ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له . ومنهم من يجعلها تعترف فيها الأعراض ، فمن ذلك تولد العالم ، يسمونه من قبل هيولى ، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع ، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال
And among them is one who says: this world came from an origin in which craft occurred. They differed: some of them make its origin clay, from which the Creator originated this world. And some people made the stars and the sun – as they run constantly – and they make them cause the growth of the world. And they make for the running a beginning by deeming impossible the coming of something from something to what has no beginning. And among them is one who makes them acknowledge accidentally occurring attributes (a'rad), and from that comes the generation of the world. They call it from before matter (hayula), and describe it as the monotheists describe the Maker. Then they invalidated that by the possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage presents two more theories about the world's origin. The first theory claims the world originated from a single primordial element, specifically clay, from which the Creator fashioned the world. The second theory involves astral influence: stars and the sun are constantly moving and cause the world's development. They posit a beginning for this motion, rejecting an infinite regress. Another subgroup within this second theory holds that the celestial bodies acknowledge accidental attributes (a'rad) in their motion, and from this the world is generated; they call this underlying matter 'hayula' and describe it similarly to how monotheists describe the Creator. However, they then invalidate this idea by noting that such matter is subject to receiving accidents and changing from one state to another, implying it cannot be eternal or divine.

Key Terms
طينة (tina/clay)
أصل (asl/origin)
صنعة (sina'a/craft)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that the world originated from clay might be mistaken as a biblical or Quranic concept, but here it is presented as a materialist theory.
Correction: This is not the Islamic account of creation; it is a philosophical/materialist theory that assumes a pre-existing substance.

Checkpoint
What did some believe to be the original element from which the world was made?
A) Fire
B) Water
C) Clay
D) Stars

Key Takeaway
The theory of an original element posits a pre-existing substance, reducing creation to mere reshaping of matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that Allah's power is limited by your circumstances—as if He can only work with the 'clay' of your current situation? How does this lesson's critique of the clay theory expose that as a subtle form of limiting Allah? What is one area of your life where you need to believe He can create something from nothing?
?
This passage shows a belief that reduces the Creator to a craftsman. In your own life, do you ever act as if Allah is merely rearranging existing pieces rather than creating entirely new possibilities? Reflect on a time you doubted His ability to bring about something unprecedented. What would it look like to trust in His absolute creative power today?

The Theory of Astral Influence and Hayula
Maturidi presents the astral influence theory and the hayula variant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astral influence theory and the subgroup that posits hayula (matter) and accidents, along with their self-invalidation due to susceptibility to change.

Core Idea
Those who attribute world-generation to stars or to a prime matter (hayula) end up contradicting themselves.

This lesson explores another ancient cosmology: the idea that the world develops due to the constant motion of stars and sun. Some among them even posited an underlying matter (hayula) that receives accidents. However, they themselves invalidated this idea because such matter would be changeable and thus not eternal or divine. Maturidi presents these views to showcase their internal inconsistencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
From 'وقوم جعلوا النجوم والشمس...' to the end. Note the two sub-views: (a) stars cause world growth, (b) a subgroup says stars have accidents and there is hayula.
2
Identify the sub-views
The first sub-view says the stars and sun run constantly and cause the development (نشوء) of the world. The second sub-view adds that these celestial bodies acknowledge accidents (أعراض) and the world is generated from that, calling the underlying matter hayula.
3
Examine the self-critique
The proponents of the hayula view themselves invalidated it by pointing out that hayula is subject to receiving accidents and changing from state to state. This means it cannot be eternal or the ultimate origin.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Even non-Muslim thinkers recognized that anything subject to change cannot be the eternal source. This aligns with the Islamic argument that the Creator must be unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول : هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه ، اختلفوا : فمنهم من يجعل أصلة طينة ، أحدث البارى منها هذا العالم . وقوم جعلوا النجوم والشمس بما كن يجرين دائبات ، ويجريان نشوء العالم . ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له . ومنهم من يجعلها تعترف فيها الأعراض ، فمن ذلك تولد العالم ، يسمونه من قبل هيولى ، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع ، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال
And among them is one who says: this world came from an origin in which craft occurred. They differed: some of them make its origin clay, from which the Creator originated this world. And some people made the stars and the sun – as they run constantly – and they make them cause the growth of the world. And they make for the running a beginning by deeming impossible the coming of something from something to what has no beginning. And among them is one who makes them acknowledge accidentally occurring attributes (a'rad), and from that comes the generation of the world. They call it from before matter (hayula), and describe it as the monotheists describe the Maker. Then they invalidated that by the possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage presents two more theories about the world's origin. The first theory claims the world originated from a single primordial element, specifically clay, from which the Creator fashioned the world. The second theory involves astral influence: stars and the sun are constantly moving and cause the world's development. They posit a beginning for this motion, rejecting an infinite regress. Another subgroup within this second theory holds that the celestial bodies acknowledge accidental attributes (a'rad) in their motion, and from this the world is generated; they call this underlying matter 'hayula' and describe it similarly to how monotheists describe the Creator. However, they then invalidate this idea by noting that such matter is subject to receiving accidents and changing from one state to another, implying it cannot be eternal or divine.

Key Terms

شمس (shams/sun)
أعراض (a'rad/accidents)
هيولى (hayula/matter)

Checkpoint
What was the self-critique mentioned by those who believed in hayula?
A) It is eternal
B) It is created
C) It accepts accidents and changes, so it cannot be the origin
D) It is the same as the Creator

Key Takeaway
The astral theory and its hayula variant collapse because changeability contradicts the required eternality of the world's origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What created thing do you rely on most for your sense of security or identity? In what way is that thing changeable, and how does this lesson challenge you to reorient that reliance toward the Unchangeable Creator?
?
The hayula theorists described matter in terms reserved for the Maker. Have you ever unknowingly attributed divine qualities (like self-sufficiency or eternality) to something created? What step can you take today to correct that in your heart?
?
This passage shows that even flawed arguments can self-destruct when they acknowledge changeability. What belief or attitude in your own life, if you honestly examined it, would invalidate itself because it rests on something transient? Will you act on that insight today?

Dualist Views: Light and Darkness as Origins
Maturidi presents two dualist accounts of cosmic origins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist cosmological view that the world originates from two principles—light and darkness—and the variations among dualist thinkers.

Core Idea
Dualists claim all good comes from light, all evil from darkness.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the dualist theory that the world originated from two eternal principles: light and darkness. This view attributes good to light and evil to darkness. Maturidi notes different versions of this theory and points out that most dualists believe the world comes about through nature rather than deliberate action. Understanding this view is essential for recognizing how early Muslim theologians engaged with competing cosmologies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two principles
Recognize that dualists posit light and darkness as the origins of all existence, with light as source of good and darkness of evil.
Note the binary assignment of moral categories.
2
Compare the two versions
Some dualists say the principles were separate then mixed; others (proponents of matter) say they were one and then separated. Understand the distinction.
The second version derives from the idea of a single primordial matter.
3
Note the role of nature
Observe that most dualists attribute the world's coming-to-be to nature (tabi'a), not to a deliberate act (fi'l). This is a key philosophical commitment.
This contrasts with theistic creation ex nihilo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول : أصله اثنان : نور وظلمة ، من النور كل خير ومن الظلمة كل شر وضار ، لكن منهم من يقول : كانا متباينين فامتزجا ، على ما مر بيانه . و على قول أصحاب الهيولى والطينة يجب أن يكونا واحدا فتفرقا ؛ إذ هي الأصل ، فصارا أصلا للشر والخير ، فبالتفريق عمل كل عمله على أن عامة هؤلاء يجعلون كون العالم بالطبيعة لا بالفعل
And among them is one who says: its origin is two: light and darkness; from light is all good, and from darkness is all evil and harm. But among them is one who says: they were separate then mixed, as previously explained. And according to the saying of the proponents of matter (hayula) and clay, they must be one and then separated; since matter is the origin, they became an origin for evil and good. So by separation, each does its work, although most of these make the world's coming-to-be by nature, not by act.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage presents the dualist view that the world originated from two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). Some dualists say these were originally separate and then mixed. The author notes that, according to the proponents of matter (hayula) and clay, these two principles must have originally been one and then separated, because matter is the ultimate origin. After separation, each principle operates according to its nature. The passage concludes by observing that most of these thinkers attribute the world's coming-to-be to nature (tabi'a) rather than to a deliberate act (fi'l).

Key Terms
نور
ظلمة
خير
شر
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is exclusively a religious concept from Zoroastrianism.
Correction: Maturidi discusses dualism as a philosophical cosmology, regardless of its religious origins.

Checkpoint
According to the dualists, what is the source of all good?
A) Darkness
B) Light
C) The mixture of both
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Dualist theories propose two eternal principles, light and darkness, as the sources of good and evil, but differ on their relationship and the mechanism of the world's origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that dualists attribute evil to an independent dark force. When you experience hardship, do you instinctively blame external factors (people, circumstances) rather than seeing it as a test from Allah or a consequence of your own actions? What would it look like to respond with complete trust in Allah's decree?
?
The passage notes that most dualists believe the world comes about by nature, not by act. In your daily life, are there moments you treat events as 'just natural' or 'random' rather than acknowledging Allah's will? How might consciously seeing everything as an act of Allah change your perspective?
?
Some dualists believed in a single origin that later split into light and darkness. Reflect: Are there beliefs or attitudes you hold that seem to have two opposing sources but actually stem from a single root in your heart? What would it take to bring that root into submission to Allah alone?

The Moral Argument for Dualism
Maturidi presents the moral argument used by dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the moral argument that some have used to justify dualism, and why it is based on an invalid analogy from human custom.

Core Idea
Judging God by human standards leads to false conclusions.

Some have argued that since good and evil exist in the world, they must come from separate origins. This argument, based on human customs of praising the good and blaming the evil, seems plausible but fails when applied to God. In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents this argument as held by the dualists and materialists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
The Arabic has some unclear parts due to manuscript issues.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument is based on an analogy from human custom: in human experience, a good being does good, an evil being does evil. The dualists apply this to God.
3
Notice the conclusion
The argument concludes that there must be two origins: one for good (light/God) and one for evil (darkness/Satan).
This leads to the dualist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : الاعتبار بالمعانى ، فقالوا : إنا نجد العالم اشتمل على نفع وضر ، وعلى خير وشر ، ثم في العرف أن فاعل الخير محمود ، ومن بنفع غيره رحيم حكيم ، وأن فاعل الشر مذموم ، ومن يضر غيره قاس سفيه ، لم يجز أن يجي من الذي هو حكيم رحيم فعل الشر أو الضرر بأحد ، بمثله في الشاهد ، ولا له السفه والقساوة ، وهذا مما ينتفع به أو يدفع الضرر عن نفسه ، فكيف لمن لا ينتفع بشيء ولا يضره شيء. على قولهم إن الحكيم فى الشاهد من يجر بفعله النفع به والضرر " من يصير غيره بلا نفع له فليس هو بحكيم. فقالوا : هذا باختلاف الأصل العالم لير نجمع كل موجود فيه إلى أصله من خير أو شر ، أو كان واحد! ١٠ فيه الجوهران فتفرقا ، فكان من كل ما يكون من مثله ، أو بما اعترضت فيه الأعراض اختلف . فرجع إلى هذا قول الدهرية المنكرة للمصانع ، والمثبتة جميعا لعدد، قسمت / الثنوية [اقولهم : الخير بجوهره نور والشر ظلمة ، والمجوس سموا [٥٥ ب ] ) الخير الله ، والشر الشيطان
And the third: consideration by moral meanings. They said: we find the world comprises benefit and harm, good and evil. Then in custom, the doer of good is praised, and one who benefits others is merciful and wise; and the doer of evil is blamed, and one who harms others is harsh and foolish. It is not possible that from the Wise, Merciful should come an act of evil or harm to anyone, like the similar in the witnessed world, nor does He possess foolishness and harshness. And this [good] is something one benefits from or repels harm from oneself. How then for one who neither benefits from anything nor is harmed by anything? According to their saying, the wise person in the witnessed world is one who acts for his own benefit or harm. One who benefits others without benefit to himself is not wise. So they said: this is due to the difference of the world's origin, so that every existent in it returns to its origin of good or evil, or it was one containing two substances that separated, so from each comes what is like it, or due to accidents that intervened, it differed. So this returns to the saying of the materialists (dahriyya) who deny the Maker, and those who affirm [two] numbers, i.e., the dualists who say: good in its essence is light and evil is darkness, and the Magians called good God and evil Satan.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage presents the moral argument used by some to support dualism. The argument proceeds from observed good and evil in the world. In human custom, a good and merciful being only does good, while evil comes from an evil being. Since God (as described by monotheists) is all-good and wise, He cannot be the source of evil. Therefore, there must be two separate origins: one for good (light) and one for evil (darkness). The author reports that this line of reasoning ultimately leads back to the dualist position of the Magians and the materialists who deny a single Maker. The argument is based on an analogy from human experience, which is invalid when applied to God, but the author does not explicitly refute it here; that comes in the next lesson.

Key Terms
اعتبار بالمعاني (moral consideration)
خير (good)
شر (evil)
حكيم (wise)
رحيم (merciful)
ثنوية (dualists)
مجوس (Magians)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because we see evil in the world, the source of creation cannot be all-good.
Correction: This argument fails because God's actions are not bound by human conventions of praise and blame; His wisdom transcends created categories.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the moral argument for dualism?
A) Scriptural texts
B) The coherence of natural phenomena
C) Human customs regarding good and evil
D) The eternity of matter

Key Takeaway
The moral argument for dualism rests on an invalid analogy from human custom to divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or see evil in the world, do you ever find yourself questioning whether God could really be all-good and all-powerful? How does this lesson's exposure of the flawed analogy between human and divine wisdom challenge you to trust God's wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
Have you ever secretly thought that the bad things in your life come from a source other than God—perhaps a devil or bad luck—while the good things come from God? What would it take for you to fully accept that every event, good or evil in appearance, is from Allah alone, as the Qur'an teaches?
?
Think of a specific hardship you faced. In that moment, did you judge God’s wisdom by human standards, expecting that a wise being would never allow that pain? How can you train your heart to say ‘Allah knows best’ without falling into the dualist mindset that separates good and evil into different origins?

Dualist Theories of Two Origins
Maturidi describes the dualist theory of two origins and two sub-views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist claim that the world originates from two principles—light (good) and darkness (evil)—and two accounts of their relationship.

Core Idea
Dualists split reality into two origins: light for good, darkness for evil.

Maturidi begins by presenting the core dualist doctrine of two origins. He then explains two variations among dualists: some say light and darkness were originally separate and later mixed, while others say they were originally one and then separated. This sets the stage for the dualist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two origins and what they produce
The passage states that light produces every good and darkness produces every evil and harm. Confirm this in the text.
3
Note the two different accounts
Some dualists say light and darkness were distinct then mixed; others say they were one then separated. Find both in the text.
The second view is associated with proponents of matter (hyle).
4
Observe the role of nature
Maturidi notes that most dualists attribute the world's existence to nature (tabiʿa), not deliberate action (fiʿl). Why might this matter?
This prepares for later critiques.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول: أصله اثنان: نور وظلمة، من النور كل خير ومن الظلمة كل شر وضار. لكن منهم من يقول: كانا متباينين فامتزجا، على ما مر بيانه. وعلى قول أصحاب الهيولى والطينة أن يكونا واحدا فتفرقا؛ إذ هي الأصل، فصارا أصلا للشر والخير، فبالتفريق عمل كل عمله على أن عامة هؤلاء يجعلون كون العالم بالطبيعة لا بالفعل. والثالث: الاعتبار بالمعاني، فقالوا: إنا نجد العالم اشتمل على نفع وضر، وعلى خير وشر. ثم في العرف أن فاعل الخير محمود، ومن بنفع غيره رحيم حكيم، وأن فاعل الشر مذموم، ومن يضر غيره قاس سفيه، لم يجز أن يجيء من الذي هو حكيم رحيم فعل الشر أو الضرر بأحد، بمثله في الشاهد، ولا له السفه والقساوة، وهذا مما ينتفع به أو يدفع الضرر عن نفسه، فكيف لمن لا ينتفع بشيء ولا يضره شيء. على قولهم إن الحكيم في الشاهد من يجري بفعله النفع به والضرر "من يصير غيره بلا نفع له فليس هو بحكيم. فقالوا: هذا باختلاف الأصل العالم لير نجمع كل موجود فيه إلى أصله من خير أو شر، أو كان واحدا فيه الجوهران فتفرقا، فكان من كل ما يكون من مثله، أو بما اعترضت فيه الأعراض اختلف. فرجع إلى هذا قول الدهرية المنكرة للمصانع، والمثبتة جميعا لعدد، قسمت الثنوية أقولهم: الخير بجوهره نور والشر ظلمة، والمجوس سموا الخير الله والشر الشيطان.
And among them are those who say: its origin is two: light and darkness, from light is every good and from darkness is every evil and harm. But among them are those who say: they were distinct then mixed, as explained earlier. And according to the proponents of matter and clay, they must have been one then separated; since it is the origin, they became an origin for evil and good, and by separation each performed its action, although the majority of these make the world's existence by nature not by act. And the third: consideration of meanings, for they said: we find the world comprises benefit and harm, and good and evil. Then customarily the doer of good is praised, and one who benefits others is merciful and wise; and the doer of evil is blamed, and one who harms others is harsh and foolish. It is not permissible that from one who is wise and merciful should come an act of evil or harm to anyone, analogous to what is witnessed, nor is foolishness and harshness attributable to Him, and this is something beneficial or warding off harm from oneself, so how about one who neither benefits nor harms anything? According to their saying that the wise in the witnessed is one who conducts benefit and harm by his act, "One who makes another without benefit for him is not wise." So they said: this is due to the difference of the world's origin, to gather every existent in it to its origin of good or evil, or it was one with two essences which separated, so from each came what is like it, or due to accidents that intervened it differed. So this returns to the saying of the materialists who deny the artificers, and those who affirm all numbers, the dualists divided [into] their sayings: good by its essence is light and evil is darkness, and the Magians called good God and evil Satan.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage presents the dualist doctrines concerning the origin of good and evil. Maturidi begins by describing the view that the world has two origins: light (source of all good) and darkness (source of all evil and harm). Some dualists say these two were originally separate and later mixed; others, following the proponents of matter and clay (hyle), say the two were originally one and then separated, becoming the sources of evil and good. Most of these groups hold that the world operates by nature, not by deliberate action. Then Maturidi presents a third line of reasoning called 'consideration of meanings' (iʿtibār bil-maʿānī). The dualists argue by analogy: in the observed world, a doer of good is praised and considered merciful and wise, while a doer of evil is blamed and considered harsh and foolish. Hence, a single wise and merciful agent cannot produce both good and evil, for that would contradict His attributes. Therefore, they conclude that the world must have two distinct origins—one producing good, the other evil—or that the world was originally one containing two essences which separated, or that differences arise from intervening accidents. This, Maturidi notes, ultimately reduces to the position of the materialists (dahriyya) who deny a Creator, and to the dualists and Magians who identify good with light/God and evil with darkness/Satan.

نور
ظلمة
خير
شر
ثنوية
هيولى
طبيعة

Checkpoint
According to the dualists, what does light produce?
A) Both good and evil
B) Only good
C) Only evil
D) Neither good nor evil

Key Takeaway
Dualists posit two eternal principles, light and darkness, as the sources of good and evil, differing on whether they were originally separate or one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists attribute every good to light and every evil to darkness. In your own life, do you ever find yourself blaming your sins on external factors (like your upbringing or environment) rather than taking full responsibility for your choices before Allah? How does this passage expose the danger of dividing the source of your actions?
?
Maturidi notes that most dualists believe the world operates by nature, not by a deliberate act. Are there areas of your life where you act as if your habits and routines are just 'natural' and beyond your control, forgetting that every moment is sustained by Allah's will and that you are accountable for your choices?
?
This passage describes a fundamental departure from tawhid by positing two eternal origins. Is there anything in your heart that you treat as a second 'origin'—a person, a desire, or a fear—that competes with Allah's sole lordship over your life? What would it take to uproot that and affirm that He alone is the source of all that happens?

The Dualists' Argument from Benefit and Harm
Maturidi presents the dualists' argument from benefit and harm (iʿtibār bil-maʿānī).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' argument that a single wise and merciful God cannot be the source of both good and evil, based on analogy with human custom.

Core Idea
In human custom, a wise and merciful being does not do evil; therefore, the world must have two origins.

After presenting the basic dualist views, Maturidi introduces a third line of reasoning based on observation of benefit and harm in the world. The dualists argue by analogy: in human experience, a doer of good is praised as wise and merciful, while a doer of evil is blamed as harsh and foolish. Therefore, a single wise and merciful being cannot produce both good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage focusing on the argument from custom
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This is the third argument (والثالث).
Note that this argument is based on human custom (عرف).
2
Identify the premise about human custom
Find where the passage describes how humans praise good doers and blame evil doers. What attributes are attached to each?
3
Understand the conclusion
The dualists conclude that a single wise and merciful being cannot commit evil or harm. Trace the logic from premise to conclusion.
4
Note the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a question: how about one who neither benefits nor harms anything? What is its significance?
This underscores the absurdity of a single wise God causing harm.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents the dualist doctrines concerning the origin of good and evil. The Dualists' Conclusion: Multiple Origins or Materialism
Maturidi concludes the dualist section with their final conclusions and his implicit critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' final conclusions and how their reasoning leads to either multiple origins or a materialistic denial of a creator.

Core Idea
Dualist reasoning ultimately reduces to either two separate essences, accidents causing differences, or pure materialism.

Maturidi concludes his presentation of dualist doctrines by summarizing their various conclusions. He notes that their arguments lead to the idea that the world's diversity comes from different origins—either from two original essences that separated and then each produced its own kind, or from accidents that intervened and caused differences. This, he says, ultimately reduces to the position of the materialists (dahriyya) who deny a creator, and to the dualists and Magians who identify good with light/God and evil with darkness/Satan.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This part contains the dualists' conclusions and Maturidi's final remark.
The Arabic has some unclear phrases, but the main ideas are discernible.
2
Identify the three possible conclusions
The dualists list three possibilities for explaining the world's diversity. Find each one in the text.
They are: tracing everything to its origin, two essences separating, or accidents causing differences.
3
Understand the reduction to materialism
Maturidi says these conclusions 'return to the saying of the materialists who deny the artificers.' What does this imply?
He is critiquing that dualism ultimately leads to atheism or polytheism.
4
Note the Magian division
The passage ends with the Magians identifying good as God and evil as Satan. How does this relate to the earlier views?
It shows the practical outcome of dualist thought.

?

?

Dualism Makes Knowledge of Wisdom Impossible
Maturidi argues that dualism makes knowledge of wisdom and folly impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that dualism leads to the impossibility of distinguishing wisdom from foolishness.

Core Idea
Dualism destroys the foundation for moral knowledge.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid refutes Manichaean dualism. Maturidi demonstrates that if good and evil arise from two independent, eternal principles (light and darkness), then human beings—being composed of both—cannot be certain whether their judgments originate from the good or evil principle. Consequently, all claims about wisdom and folly become unreliable. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist position
First, recognize the dualist claim that good and evil come from separate essences: the essence of light (good) and the essence of darkness (evil). This is the starting point of Maturidi's critique.
Manichaeans held that good and evil are two coeternal principles.
2
See the problem with human nature
According to dualism, every human being is a mixture of both essences. Therefore, any action or judgment a person makes could be influenced by either principle.
Maturidi says 'kull al-insān 'indahum mashūb min al-amrayn' – every human is mixed from the two matters.
3
Recognize the epistemic consequence
Because a person is a mixture, one cannot determine whether their testimony comes from the truthful light or the false darkness. This makes their claims about wisdom and folly unreliable.
If a person is from the essence of darkness, everything they say is false; if from light, everything is true—but we don't know which.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, under dualism, no one can be trusted to know wisdom from folly, and the entire framework for moral knowledge collapses.
Maturidi ends with 'lā yuqāl' – 'so it is not known' – highlighting the uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معرفة حقيقة الحكمة والسفه ; إذ من مذهبهم أن لا يرون بجوهر الشر إلا الشر ويجوهر الخير إلا الخير , ثم لا يدرى فيها سموه سفها أو حكمة إنه فعل الشر أو فعل الخير , وكل الإنسان عندهم مشوب من الأمرين , يرى بكل واحد خلاف ما يرى بالآخر , فلعله رأى الحكمة سفها والسفه حكمة , ثم لا يوثق بقوله لأنه خير فهو من جوهر الظلمة كذب كله ومن جوهر النور صدق كله فلا يدرى بأى جوهرين ينطق , ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Knowing the reality of wisdom and foolishness; because it is their doctrine that they do not see in the essence of evil except evil and in the essence of good except good, then it is not known whether they have called it foolishness or wisdom, whether it is an act of evil or an act of good. And every human being according to them is mixed from the two principles, seeing with each one contrary to what he sees with the other. Perhaps he saw wisdom as foolishness and foolishness as wisdom. Then his statement is not reliable, because if he is good, he is from the essence of darkness, all lies; and if from the essence of light, all truth; so it is not known by which of the two essences he speaks. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Manichaean dualist doctrine that good and evil arise from two separate eternal principles (light and darkness). Maturidi begins by pointing out that according to dualism, a human being is composed of both essences. Consequently, when a person performs an act, it is impossible to determine whether it arises from the light principle (good) or the darkness principle (evil). If a person claims to know wisdom from foolishness, his own judgment is suspect because he may be speaking from either essence. Thus, the dualist position makes any reliable knowledge of wisdom and folly impossible, leading to self-contradiction.

Key Terms
حكمة
سفه
جوهر
نور
ظلمة
مشوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is rejecting the distinction between good and evil itself.
Correction: Maturidi is not rejecting the distinction; he is showing that dualism cannot ground that distinction reliably, leading to epistemic chaos.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the testimony of a human being unreliable under dualism?
A) Because humans are made of both light and darkness, so it is unknown which essence is speaking.
B) Because humans always lie.
C) Because the light essence only speaks falsehood.
D) Because the darkness essence only speaks truth.

Key Takeaway
Dualism, by positing two independent sources of good and evil, makes it impossible to have reliable knowledge of wisdom and folly.

Loss of Hope and Fear in Dualism
Maturidi presents the first argument against dualism: loss of hope and fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that dualism destroys the basis for hope and fear and nullifies the benefit of distinguishing wisdom from foolishness.

Core Idea
Dualism removes hope and fear because no principle can cause harm or benefit.

Maturidi begins his critique of dualism by pointing out a practical consequence: if light and darkness are independent and neither can affect the other, then there is no basis for hope or fear. Since hope and fear are tied to the possibility of benefit or harm, their absence makes the distinction between wisdom and foolishness meaningless. This passage lays out that initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'since neither...', then the conclusion 'so hope and fear are cut off'.
2
Identify the premise
Identify the dualist claim that neither principle can cause harm or benefit to the other. Maturidi takes this as given.
3
Connect to hope and fear
Reflect on why hope and fear require the possibility of benefit and harm. Without that possibility, these emotions are irrational.
4
Note the lost benefit
The passage says the benefit of knowing wisdom and foolishness is lost. Why? Because wisdom is about placing things properly, which implies choice and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للاخر قدرة على النفع , فذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه . ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا حال والسفه بالطبع , والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه والسفه وضع كل شيء لا في غير موضعه , ومحال وصف ذي طبع به ; إذ هو اختيار , والنور عندهم يعلم ما السفه فيحذره ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة , والجهل بمائية الشيء وبالوضع له شره فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل ثم القدرة والعجز بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه نفسه ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به , فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوبا بالشر وجوهر الظلمة لا خير فيه , فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير , والذي هو خير لم يعرف البشر والسفه , فكيف هذا الذي يولد عن جوهر الخير بعد غلبة الشر عليه الخير والشر .
Then, since neither of the two has power over harm, nor the other power over benefit, so hope and fear are both cut off, and the benefit of knowing wisdom and foolishness is lost. Then, since each of the two essences acts by nature, so knowledge of wisdom occurs by nature and foolishness by nature, and wisdom is placing everything in its proper place, and foolishness is placing everything not in its proper place, and it is impossible to describe something with nature thereby, for it is choice. And light according to them knows what foolishness is and so guards against it, but darkness does not know what wisdom is. And ignorance of the quiddity of a thing and of its proper place is its evil. So the essence of light according to them becomes that in which knowledge and ignorance have gathered, then power and incapacity, such that it cannot avert foolishness from itself nor prevent darkness from harming it. So the essence of good according to them becomes mixed with evil, and the essence of darkness has no good in it. Hence their statement necessitates the dominance of evil over good. And that which is good did not know evil and foolishness, so how can this which is born from the essence of good after the dominance of evil over it be good and evil?
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage presents two consecutive arguments against dualism. First, Maturidi notes that if neither principle can cause harm or benefit, then there is no basis for hope or fear, and the practical benefit of distinguishing wisdom from folly is lost. Second, if both essences act by nature rather than choice, then wisdom and foolishness cannot be attributed to them, because these concepts require voluntary choice. Moreover, the light principle, though it knows foolishness, cannot prevent itself from being affected by darkness due to its nature, leading to a mixture of knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity. Consequently, the light essence becomes mixed with evil, and darkness remains wholly evil. This results in the conclusion that evil dominates good, which contradicts the dualist claim of two independent principles.

Key Terms
حكمة
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim follows from dualism's claim that neither principle can cause harm or benefit?
A) Hope and fear are strengthened
B) Hope and fear are cut off
C) Wisdom becomes more valuable
D) Darkness becomes good

Key Takeaway
Dualism destroys the foundation of hope and fear and makes moral knowledge useless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that no power could bring you ultimate benefit or ultimate harm, would your hope in Allah and fear of His punishment remain? How would that change your daily prayers and reliance on Him?
?
The passage says the benefit of knowing wisdom is lost when there is no power behind it. In what areas of your life do you act as if moral knowledge has no practical consequence—perhaps by neglecting a sin you consider small? Are you living as though wisdom is pointless?
?
Dualism removes the foundation for hope and fear. Do you ever behave as if Allah has no power over your life—worrying excessively about provision or feeling safe from His plan? That mindset is a form of practical dualism. What would it take to replace it with tawakkul (trust) and khashyah (awe)?

The Problem of Natural Causation and the Mixture of Good and Evil
Maturidi argues that natural causation leads to contradictions and mixture of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if the two principles act by nature, then wisdom and foolishness cannot be properly attributed, and the essence of good becomes mixed with evil.

Core Idea
If both essences act by nature, then neither can truly be wise or foolish, and good becomes contaminated with evil.

Maturidi continues his critique by examining the dualist claim that each essence acts by nature, not by choice. He shows this leads to contradictions: wisdom and foolishness require voluntary choice, so they cannot be properties of natural beings. Moreover, the light principle, though it knows foolishness, cannot prevent itself from being harmed by darkness, resulting in a mixture of good and evil. This passage exposes the internal inconsistency of dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the use of 'then' to connect the second argument.
2
Understand the nature vs. choice distinction
Maturidi argues that wisdom and foolishness are about placing things properly or improperly, which implies choice. Natural action cannot be described as wise or foolish.
3
Follow the reductio on light
Light knows foolishness but cannot avoid it due to its nature, so it becomes a mixture of knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity.
4
See the conclusion
Ultimately, evil dominates good, contradicting the dualist claim of two independent principles. Good is mixed with evil, and darkness remains purely evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للاخر قدرة على النفع , فذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه . ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا حال والسفه بالطبع , والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه والسفه وضع كل شيء لا في غير موضعه , ومحال وصف ذي طبع به ; إذ هو اختيار , والنور عندهم يعلم ما السفه فيحذره ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة , والجهل بمائية الشيء وبالوضع له شره فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل ثم القدرة والعجز بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه نفسه ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به , فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوبا بالشر وجوهر الظلمة لا خير فيه , فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير , والذي هو خير لم يعرف البشر والسفه , فكيف هذا الذي يولد عن جوهر الخير بعد غلبة الشر عليه الخير والشر .
Then, since neither of the two has power over harm, nor the other power over benefit, so hope and fear are both cut off, and the benefit of knowing wisdom and foolishness is lost. Then, since each of the two essences acts by nature, so knowledge of wisdom occurs by nature and foolishness by nature, and wisdom is placing everything in its proper place, and foolishness is placing everything not in its proper place, and it is impossible to describe something with nature thereby, for it is choice. And light according to them knows what foolishness is and so guards against it, but darkness does not know what wisdom is. So the essence of good according to them becomes mixed with evil, and the essence of darkness has no good in it. Hence their statement necessitates the dominance of evil over good. And that which is good did not know evil and foolishness, so how can this which is born from the essence of good after the dominance of evil over it be good and evil?
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage presents two consecutive arguments against dualism. First, Maturidi notes that if neither principle can cause harm or benefit, then there is no basis for hope or fear, and the practical benefit of distinguishing wisdom from folly is lost. Second, if both essences act by nature rather than choice, then wisdom and foolishness cannot be attributed to them, because these concepts require voluntary choice. Moreover, the light principle, though it knows foolishness, cannot prevent itself from being affected by darkness due to its nature, leading to a mixture of knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity. Consequently, the light essence becomes mixed with evil, and darkness remains wholly evil. This results in the conclusion that evil dominates good, which contradicts the dualist claim of two independent principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Maturidi is arguing for determinism here.
Correction: In fact, he is using a reductio ad absurdum: assuming dualism leads to the absurd conclusion that good is mixed with evil, so dualism must be false.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to the essence of light in the dualist view?
A) It remains purely good
B) It becomes mixed with evil
C) It gains power over darkness
D) It becomes ignorant

Key Takeaway
If both principles act by nature, then good becomes contaminated with evil, undermining the dualist claim of two pure principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted out of sheer habit (like nature) rather than conscious choice, and later realized you couldn't prevent the harm that followed? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of leaving the good and evil in your soul unexamined?
?
The argument says that light knows foolishness but cannot stop it from being harmed. Where in your life do you know what is wrong yet feel powerless to change it? How does this lesson challenge your excuse of 'I can't help it'?
?
If pure good cannot coexist with pure evil without becoming mixed, what does that mean for how you allow the darkness in your own heart to mingle with the good? Are you guarding against the evil within, or do you assume you remain pure? What concrete step will you take today to separate the two?

Impossibility of Two All-Powerful Creators
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing two creators would lead to ignorance or evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical dilemma that refutes the possibility of two independent, all-powerful creators.

Core Idea
Two all-powerful creators logically lead to ignorance or evil.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument against those who claim there are two creators, such as light and darkness. He sets up a dilemma that forces the dualist into a contradiction. Let's read the passage and see how the argument unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Focus on the logical flow of the argument.
The passage is a single, tightly reasoned sentence.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that the passage presents two possibilities for each creator: either it knows the way to prevent the other and is able, or it does not know and is unable. Trace how each leads to a contradiction.
3
Examine the consequences
If the creator does not know or cannot prevent, ignorance and incapacity are attributed to it. If it knows and can prevent but does not, it is evil. Both outcomes contradict the nature of a divine creator.
The dualists claimed light is good, but this argument shows it must be either flawed or evil.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Ask yourself: does this argument completely rule out two creators? Why does Maturidi consider it decisive? Think about the logic of the dilemma.
The dilemma shows that belief in two creators is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يقول بأن كل واحد منها خالق قادر فإنه لا يخلو كل واحد منها من يعلم الوجه الذي يمنع الآخر عن عمله أو لا يقدر عليه أو لا , فإن لم يعلم ولم يقدر اجتمع في النور الجهل والعجز , وفي ذلك بطلان السبب الذي له قالوا باثنين , وإن علم وقدر ثم لم يعمل في المنع لحقه وصف الشر .
And whoever says that each of them is a creator, powerful — then each of them cannot be free from knowing the way by which it prevents the other from its action, or being unable to do so, or not. If it does not know and is unable, then ignorance and incapacity come together in the light, and in that is the invalidation of the reason for which they affirmed two. And if it knows and is able, then does not act in prevention, the attribute of evil attaches to it.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-argument against the dualist claim that both light and darkness are creators and powerful. Maturidi presents a dilemma: either each knows how to prevent the other and is able to do so, or not. If it does not know or is unable, then the light essence (which is supposed to be pure good) would be characterized by ignorance and incapacity, undermining the reason for positing two principles. If it does know and is able, then its failure to prevent evil makes it evil itself (since allowing evil knowingly is evil). Thus, the dualist position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
خالق
قادر
علم
قدرة
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that two equally powerful divine beings can coexist as creators of good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this leads to either ignorance/incapacity or evil in the light, which is a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the second horn of Maturidi's dilemma regarding two powerful creators?
A) If each is all-knowing and all-powerful, it would prevent the other, so it must be evil.
B) If each is ignorant and weak, then they are not creators.
C) If each is good, they would cooperate.
D) If each is evil, they would destroy each other.

Key Takeaway
Two all-powerful creators are logically impossible because the only two possibilities both lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unjust, do you ever mentally divide the world into a good power and an evil power, even if only subconsciously? How does Maturidi's argument expose that such a division is logically impossible and actually undermines your trust in Allah's oneness? This week, when a test comes, catch that tendency and consciously affirm that Allah alone is in control, with no evil rival.
?
What 'prevention' have you felt God should have done but didn't? How does the dilemma—either He didn't know/able or He chose not to—force you to confront either His attributes or your own understanding of good and evil? Do you dare to question your own criteria for what is good rather than implying a shortcoming in Him?
?
The light, if it knows and is able but does not prevent evil, becomes evil itself. How does this challenge your own responsibility? Where are you failing to prevent evil when you have the knowledge and ability, and what does that say about your own character? Commit to one concrete action this week to prevent a wrong you have been tolerating.

Objection: Afflictions Only in Witnessed World
Maturidi refutes the dualist excuse that afflictions are only in the witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualist excuse that afflictions only occur in the physical world by turning their argument against themselves.

Core Idea
If mixing causes problems, then the very creation of two principles is problematic.

In this brief exchange, Maturidi addresses a dualist objection that tries to limit the problem of conflict and mixture to the physical world. The dualist claims that afflictions (آفات) explain why we see good and evil mixed in the witnessed realm, but Maturidi points out that this reasoning actually undermines their entire position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist objection
The dualist argues that contradictions only appear in the physical world because of afflictions that mix the two principles. Read the first part of the passage carefully.
2
See Maturidi's rebuttal
Maturidi replies that the same issue applies to the creation of the two kinds (the principles themselves). If mixing due to afflictions is problematic, then creating two distinct principles in the first place is equally problematic, if not more so.
3
Reflect on the implication
Notice that Maturidi does not deny afflictions; he uses the dualist's own logic to show that their position is self-defeating. The existence of afflictions cannot rescue dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : ذلك في الشاهد لثبوت الآفات , فمثله في جميع ما أنكر من الحكمة في خلق النوعين , ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If they say: 'That is in the witnessed world due to the existence of afflictions,' then the same applies to all that you denied of wisdom in the creation of the two kinds. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This is a brief objection-and-answer. The dualist might argue that the contradictions Maturidi pointed out (like mixture of good and evil, ignorance, etc.) only occur in the physical, witnessed world because of afflictions (آفات) that mix the two principles. Maturidi replies that the same reasoning applies to the very creation of the two kinds (i.e., the two principles themselves) in the unseen realm. Thus, the objection does not rescue dualism; rather, it implicates the principles themselves.

Key Terms
شاهد
آفة
نوعان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi denies that afflictions exist in the physical world.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny afflictions; he uses the dualist's own premise to show that the existence of two principles is equally problematic.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the dualist claim that contradictions only occur due to afflictions in the physical world?
A) He denies afflictions exist.
B) He says the same reasoning applies to the creation of the two principles themselves.
C) He accepts the excuse.
D) He argues afflictions are from light.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the dualist by showing that the problem of mixture applies equally to the very creation of the two principles, making the excuse about afflictions self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made an excuse for your own shortcomings by shifting the blame to external circumstances, just as the dualist shifted the problem to the witnessed world? What would it mean for you to accept responsibility instead?
?
The dualist tried to compartmentalize the problem to one realm. In your life, where do you compartmentalize your faith, hoping that certain areas are exempt from Allah's wisdom? How would Maturidi's refutation challenge you to bring every aspect under tawhid?
?
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' When you face afflictions, do you truly rely on Allah's power, or do you seek to explain them away with excuses? What would change if you internalized that all power belongs to Him?

The Logical Incoherence of Dualism from Repentance
Maturidi examines three possibilities for attributing repentance and sin to light or darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the human experience of repentance and moral change refutes dualist claims about light and darkness.

Core Idea
Repentance shows that light cannot be purely good if it produces both sin and remorse.

Imam Maturidi begins with an undeniable human experience: people acknowledge ignorance, do good after evil, and regret sins. He then asks dualists—who believe in two eternal principles of light and darkness—to explain this phenomenon. The result is a logical dead end that exposes the incoherence of dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three possibilities Maturidi examines.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Maturidi explores three scenarios: both repentance and sin from light; repentance from light, sin from darkness; both from darkness. Find each in the text.
Each possibility is introduced by 'فإما أن' or 'أو نجعل' or 'وإما أن'.
3
Understand why each leads to contradiction
For each possibility, Maturidi shows it contradicts the dualist's own claims about light and darkness. Trace his reasoning step by step.
Focus on why attributing both to light makes light contain evil, etc.
4
Connect to the refutation of dualism
Since every possibility fails, dualism cannot account for the simple fact of repentance. This is a powerful argument from observed human behavior.
Reflect on how this argument uses everyday experience to challenge a metaphysical system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد , وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة , وبالندم بعد ذنب , وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل ; للوجود في الشاهد . فإما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء , فبطل قوله بالاثنين لهذا الوجه , أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظلمة , والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور فيكون ذلك كذبا وتخزيا واهتماما , وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة فقد أثبته للنور , ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه . وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر . وأيضا أن النور لا يخلو من أن يحل بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا , فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور , وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله في فعل الشر والضرر : إنه القسوة والشدة , لا الرحمة . وذلك في القول باثنين . بم يقال له ؟
That there must be acknowledgment after ignorance in the witnessed world, and beneficence after injury, and regret after sin, and acknowledgment of injury after reason, due to their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both things from light, then from it come ignorance, injury, sin, foolishness, and everything, so his statement of duality is invalidated in this respect; or we make injury, foolishness, and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgment, beneficence, and regret from light, then that would be lying, shame, and concern, and all that according to him is from the act of darkness, so he has affirmed it for light. Then acknowledgment of what was not is lying and foolishness. Or both are from darkness, then from it come good and evil. Also, light cannot be free from whether evil befalls its allies and it grieves over it or not. If it is concerned and grieves, his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalidated; and if it does not grieve, his statement about the act of evil and harm — that it is harshness and severity, not mercy — is invalidated. That is in the statement of two. What can be said to it?
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage presents multiple arguments from observable human experiences of repentance, regret, and emotional change. Maturidi argues that in the witnessed world, we see people acknowledge wrongdoing after ignorance, do good after evil, regret sins, etc. Dualists attribute such changes to the mixture of light and darkness. Maturidi examines the possibilities: if both good and bad actions come from light, then light contains ignorance and evil, contradicting its pure nature. If bad actions come from darkness but good acknowledgments from light, then light would be involved in lies and shame (since acknowledging something that never happened is falsehood). If both come from darkness, then darkness would produce good as well, contradicting its pure evil nature. Moreover, if light feels grief for its allies (the good people who suffer), then it experiences sorrow, contradicting its supposed pure joy. If it does not grieve, then it is harsh and unmerciful. Both alternatives are unacceptable for the dualist conception of light as pure good and joy. Thus, dualism is incoherent.

Key Terms
إقرار
ندم
إساءة
ذنب
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Maturidi is denying that genuine repentance occurs or that humans have free will.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying repentance; he is using its occurrence as a fact to show that dualism's categories cannot explain it. This argument is about the logical coherence of dualism, not about the reality of repentance.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what problem arises if we attribute both repentance and sin to light?
A) Light would be purely good.
B) Light would contain ignorance and evil.
C) Light would be joyful.
D) Light would be powerful.

Key Takeaway
The reality of repentance forces dualists into a logical trilemma, each option contradicting their own premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin and then repent, do you sometimes feel that the sin was from your weakness (or even from shaytan) and the repentance was from Allah? Maturidi shows this dualistic thinking is incoherent. In light of this, how must you now take full responsibility for both your sin and your turning back to Allah? What will you do to own both completely before Allah?
?
The argument shows that repentance proves both good and evil come from a single source. Do you live as if your good deeds are from your own effort and your bad deeds are from external causes? How has this lesson exposed that hidden shirk in your heart? What concrete step will you take today to attribute all that happens to Allah alone?

The Emotional Contradiction in the Dualist Conception of Light
Maturidi uses emotional change to refute dualism about light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the emotional nature of light—whether it can feel grief or not—to show an internal contradiction in dualism.

Core Idea
If light feels grief, it cannot be all joy; if it does not, it cannot be merciful—both contradict dualist claims.

Imam Maturidi continues his refutation by examining the dualist claim that light is pure joy and pleasure. He asks a simple question: does light grieve when evil befalls its allies? The answer leads to a dilemma that carves out another inconsistency in the dualist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two alternatives Maturidi presents.
2
Understand the dilemma
Maturidi poses a conditional: either light grieves over its allies' suffering or it does not. Both options conflict with dualist descriptions of light.
The dualists claim light is all pleasure and joy, and also that light is not harsh or severe.
3
Analyze each horn
If light grieves, then it experiences sorrow, so it cannot be all pleasure and joy. If it does not grieve, then it is indifferent to suffering, which contradicts the claim that it is merciful and not harsh.
This shows that dualism cannot consistently describe light's nature.
4
Connect to the overall refutation
This argument adds to the previous one, demonstrating multiple inconsistencies in dualism from different angles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد , وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة , وبالندم بعد ذنب , وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل ; للوجود في الشاهد . فإما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء , فبطل قوله بالاثنين لهذا الوجه , أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظلمة , والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور فيكون ذلك كذبا وتخزيا واهتماما , وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة فقد أثبته للنور , ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه . وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر . وأيضا أن النور لا يخلو من أن يحل بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا , فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور , وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله في فعل الشر والضرر : إنه القسوة والشدة , لا الرحمة . وذلك في القول باثنين . بم يقال له ؟
That there must be acknowledgment after ignorance in the witnessed world, and beneficence after injury, and regret after sin, and acknowledgment of injury after reason, due to their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both things from light, then from it come ignorance, injury, sin, foolishness, and everything, so his statement of duality is invalidated in this respect; or we make injury, foolishness, and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgment, beneficence, and regret from light, then that would be lying, shame, and concern, and all that according to him is from the act of darkness, so he has affirmed it for light. Then acknowledgment of what was not is lying and foolishness. Or both are from darkness, then from it come good and evil. Also, light cannot be free from whether evil befalls its allies and it grieves over it or not. If it is concerned and grieves, his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalidated; and if it does not grieve, his statement about the act of evil and harm — that it is harshness and severity, not mercy — is invalidated. That is in the statement of two. What can be said to it?

Commentary
This passage presents multiple arguments from observable human experiences of repentance, regret, and emotional change. Maturidi argues that in the witnessed world, we see people acknowledge wrongdoing after ignorance, do good after evil, regret sins, etc. Dualists attribute such changes to the mixture of light and darkness. Maturidi examines the possibilities: if both good and bad actions come from light, then light contains ignorance and evil, contradicting its pure nature. If bad actions come from darkness but good acknowledgments from light, then light would be involved in lies and shame (since acknowledging something that never happened is falsehood). If both come from darkness, then darkness would produce good as well, contradicting its pure evil nature. Moreover, if light feels grief for its allies (the good people who suffer), then it experiences sorrow, contradicting its supposed pure joy. If it does not grieve, then it is harsh and unmerciful. Both alternatives are unacceptable for the dualist conception of light as pure good and joy. Thus, dualism is incoherent.

Correction: Maturidi is not asserting that light literally has emotions; he is using the dualists' own descriptions of light's nature (pleasure, joy, mercy) to show internal contradictions.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say happens to the dualist claim if light does NOT grieve over evil befalling its allies?
A) Light would be all pleasure and joy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Maturidi's dilemma forces a choice: either light grieves (contradicting its joy) or it does not (contradicting its mercy). Is there an area in your faith where you are avoiding a difficult choice between two truths about God? What would it cost you to fully accept both?
?
When evil befalls someone you love, do you sometimes feel that God is indifferent? The dualists' light had to either grieve or be harsh. What does this teach you about the kind of relationship you seek with God—one that can handle your honest questions about suffering?
