The Flaw in Dualism: Light's Ignorance and Incapacity
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing light's imperfection and its inevitable relation to evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's initial refutation of dualism: if light has ignorance and incapacity, the basis for two eternal principles collapses, and the relationship between light and darkness inevitably implies evil.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses because its own first principle (light) cannot escape being the source of evil.

Maturidi begins his refutation by examining the dualists' own claim that light is a principle of good. He argues that if light can be ignorant or incapable, then it cannot be the perfect good they imagine. Moreover, whether light opposes darkness or befriends it, the outcome is evil. This sets the stage for dismantling the dualist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the first few lines where Maturidi states that if ignorance and incapacity are found in light, then the reason for two principles is invalid.
Notice how he directly attacks the foundation of dualism.
2
Understand the dilemma of hostility
Maturidi then presents two scenarios: light either opposes darkness or not. Work through each scenario and see why both lead to evil.
This is a classic logical dilemma.
3
Reflect on why this refutes dualism
Consider: if light itself is involved in evil, then the dualist's distinction between good (light) and evil (darkness) breaks down.
This ties back to the opening statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب التوحيد يقدر اجتمع فى النور الجهل والعجز ، وفى ذلك بطلان السبب الذي له قالوا باثنين ، وإن علم وقدر ثم لم يعمل في المنع لحقه وصف الشر ثم فإن لا يخلو النون من أن يعادي الظلمة أو لا ، ويحب تشاغله أو لا ، كان لا يعادى ويحب فذلك شر ؛ لأن ترك عداوة العدو والمحبة له شر ، وإن كان يعاديه ويبغضه فالعداوة والبغض شرا فى المعروف من الشاهد الشاهد . فإن قال : ذلك فى الشاهد لثبوت الآفات ، فمثله فى جميع ما أنكر من الحكمة في خلق النوعين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على حقا بعد شيء حقا أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد ، وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة ، وبالندم بعد ذنب ، وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل، وكذلك باعتقاد أن اعتقده باطلا ؛ للوجود فى الشاهد . فاما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء ، فبطل قوله بالاثنين [٥٦ ب] لهذا الوجه ، أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظلمة ، والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور فيكون ذلك كذبا وتخزيا واهتماما ، وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة فقد أثبته للنور ، ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه . وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر . وأيضا أن النور لا يخلو. أن للشر ، يحل بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا ، من يہم فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور ، وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله فى فعل الشر والضرر : إنه القسوة والشدة ، لا الرحمة . وذلك فى القول باثنين . بم يقال له ؟
Kitab al-Tawhid. If ignorance and incapacity are found in the light, then the reason for which they said there are two (principles) is invalid. And if it knows and is able but then does not act to prevent, the attribute of evil applies to it. Then the light must either be hostile to the darkness or not, and love its occupation or not. If it is not hostile and loves, that is evil, because leaving enmity to the enemy and loving him is evil. If it is hostile and hates it, then enmity and hatred are evil in what is known from the witnessed world. If they say: That (occurs) in the witnessed world because of the presence of afflictions. Then the same applies to all that they deny of wisdom in creating the two types. And there is no power except with Allah. Truly, after something true, it is necessary to acknowledge knowledge after ignorance in the witnessed world, and goodness after evil, and regret after sin, and acknowledgment of evil after reason, and likewise believing that what one believed is false, because of their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both matters from the light, so that ignorance, evil, sin, folly and everything come from it, then his statement of two (principles) is invalid for this reason; or we make evil, folly and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgment, goodness and regret from light, then that would be lying, disgrace and concern, and all that according to him is from the action of darkness, so he has attributed it to light, and then acknowledgment of what did not happen is lying and folly. Or both are from darkness, then from it come good and evil. Also, the light must either be grieved by evil that happens to its friends and be saddened or not. If it is concerned and saddened, his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalid. If it is not saddened, his statement about doing evil and harm that it is harshness and severity, not mercy, is invalid. That is concerning the statement of two (principles). What is said to him?
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist belief in two eternal principles, light and darkness. Maturidi begins by arguing that if ignorance and incapacity are present in light, then the very reason for postulating a second principle (to account for evil) is undermined. He then examines the relationship between light and darkness: if light is not hostile to darkness and even loves it, that is evil; if it is hostile, that hostility itself is evil in the witnessed world. He anticipates the dualist objection that such judgments apply only in the witnessed world due to afflictions, but counters that the same logic applies to their denial of wisdom in creation. He then presents a dilemma: either both good and evil come from light (which would make light evil), or evil comes from darkness and good from light (which would attribute lying and folly to light), or both come from darkness (which would mean darkness produces good as well). Finally, he considers whether light is affected by evil happening to its friends: if it is saddened, it is not pure joy; if it is not saddened, then its evil actions are not merciful. Thus the dualist position is internally inconsistent.

Key Terms
النور
الظلمة
الجهل
العجز
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is only refuted by scripture, not logic.
Correction: Maturidi uses rational argument to show internal inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if light is not hostile to darkness and loves it, what is the result?
A) That is good because light is merciful.
B) That is evil because leaving enmity and loving the enemy is evil.
C) That proves light is all-powerful.
D) That shows light is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that whether light is perfect or imperfect, it cannot avoid being associated with evil, thus undermining the dualist claim of a separate good principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil in the world and do nothing to oppose it, do you consider yourself innocent? How does Maturidi's argument that light's inaction is itself evil expose the flaw in that comfort? Will you from today actively seek to prevent evil you can, knowing that neutrality is complicity?
?
If you claim to follow the 'light' (truth), are you truly hostile to the darkness (falsehood) in your own life, or have you made peace with some sins? According to this passage, what does your tolerance of certain evils say about your claim to be on the side of good?
?
Do you ever find yourself subtly believing that God is responsible only for good and not for evil, as if evil has a separate source? How does this passage root out that dualistic thinking and demand that you accept God's sole sovereignty over all that happens, even the difficult parts?

The Witnessed World Objection and Its Refutation
Maturidi refutes the objection by demanding consistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi counters the dualist objection that his arguments only apply to the witnessed world, not to the eternal realm.

Core Idea
The dualist cannot selectively appeal to the witnessed world without consistency.

The dualist might object that Maturidi's previous reasoning about hostility and evil only applies to the contingent, witnessed world, not to the eternal principle of light. Maturidi responds by turning the objection back on them: if they accept that afflictions cause such judgments in the witnessed world, then they must also accept that the same logic applies to their denial of wisdom in creating the two types. He then lists examples from the witnessed world to establish a pattern.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Maturidi's initial response
The dualist claims that Maturidi's reasoning only holds in the witnessed world. Read how Maturidi immediately applies the same standard to their denial of wisdom.
2
Study the list of witnessed-world examples
Maturidi lists several cases where knowledge follows ignorance, goodness follows evil, etc. Notice how each example demonstrates a necessary sequence.
These examples are meant to show a pattern that must be consistent.
3
Understand the implication
If the dualist accepts that these patterns exist in the witnessed world, they cannot deny them in the eternal realm without inconsistency.
This is a powerful counter-argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Triple Dilemma: All Options Refute Dualism
Maturidi presents a three-pronged dilemma and a final grief argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities for the origin of good and evil and how each refutes the dualist position.

Core Idea
Whether good and evil come from light, darkness, or both, dualism is contradictory.

Maturidi now presents a decisive dilemma with three branches. He considers all possible sources of good and evil: both from light, good from light and evil from darkness, or both from darkness. Each option leads to a contradiction or absurdity. He then adds a final argument about light's grief, which further undermines the dualist's characterization of light as pure joy.

Guided Path
1
Examine the three options
Maturidi lists three possibilities: (1) both good and evil from light, (2) evil from darkness and good from light, (3) both from darkness. Read each and see the consequences.
This is a classic trilemma.
2
Understand the implications
For each option, Maturidi shows why it is problematic: if both from light, light is evil; if separate, then light is associated with lying and folly; if both from darkness, then darkness produces good.
Each leads to an inconsistency.
3
Consider the grief argument
Finally, Maturidi asks whether light is grieved by evil to its friends. If yes, light is not pure joy; if no, light's actions are not merciful.
This further challenges the dualist view of light.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب التوحيد يقدر اجتمع فى النور الجهل والعجز ، وفى ذلك بطلان السبب الذي له قالوا باثنين ، وإن علم وقدر ثم لم يعمل في المنع لحقه وصف الشر ثم فإن لا يخلو النون من أن يعادي الظلمة أو لا ، ويحب تشاغله أو لا ، كان لا يعادى ويحب فذلك شر ؛ لأن ترك عداوة العدو والمحبة له شر ، وإن كان يعاديه ويبغضه فالعداوة والبغض شرا فى المعروف من الشاهد الشاهد . فإن قال : ذلك فى الشاهد لثبوت الآفات ، فمثله فى جميع ما أنكر من الحكمة في خلق النوعين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على حقا بعد شيء حقا أنه لا بد من الإقرار بعلم بعد الجهل في الشاهد ، وبالإحسان بعد الإساءة ، وبالندم بعد ذنب ، وبالإقرار بالإساءة بعد العقل، وكذلك باعتقاد أن اعتقده باطلا ؛ للوجود فى الشاهد . فاما أن نجعل الأمرين من النور فيكون منه الجهل والإساءة والذنب والسفه وكل شيء ، فبطل قوله بالاثنين [٥٦ ب] لهذا الوجه ، أو نجعل الإساءة والسفه والجهل من الظلمة ، والإقرار والإحسان والندامة من النور فيكون ذلك كذبا وتخزيا واهتماما ، وكل ذلك عنده من فعل الظلمة فقد أثبته للنور ، ثم الإقرار بما لم يكن كذب وسفه . وإما أن يكونا من الظلمة فيكون منها خير وشر . وأيضا أن النور لا يخلو. أن للشر ، يحل بأوليائه ويحزن عليه أو لا ، من يہم فإن اهتم وحزن بطل قوله : هو كله لذة وسرور ، وإن لم يحزن بطل قوله فى فعل الشر والضرر : إنه القسوة والشدة ، لا الرحمة . وذلك فى القول باثنين . بم يقال له ؟
Kitab al-Tawhid. If ignorance and incapacity are found in the light, then the reason for which they said there are two (principles) is invalid. And if it knows and is able but then does not act to prevent, the attribute of evil applies to it. Then the light must either be hostile to the darkness or not, and love its occupation or not. If it is not hostile and loves, that is evil, because leaving enmity to the enemy and loving him is evil. If it is hostile and hates it, then enmity and hatred are evil in what is known from the witnessed world. If they say: That (occurs) in the witnessed world because of the presence of afflictions. Then the same applies to all that they deny of wisdom in creating the two types. And there is no power except with Allah. Truly, after something true, it is necessary to acknowledge knowledge after ignorance in the witnessed world, and goodness after evil, and regret after sin, and acknowledgment of evil after reason, and likewise believing that what one believed is false, because of their existence in the witnessed world. So either we make both matters from the light, so that ignorance, evil, sin, folly and everything come from it, then his statement of two (principles) is invalid for this reason; or we make evil, folly and ignorance from darkness, and acknowledgment, goodness and regret from light, then that would be lying, disgrace and concern, and all that according to him is from the action of darkness, so he has attributed it to light, and then acknowledgment of what did not happen is lying and folly. Or both are from darkness, then from it come good and evil. Also, the light must either be grieved by evil that happens to its friends and be saddened or not. If it is concerned and saddened, his statement that it is all pleasure and joy is invalid. If it is not saddened, his statement about doing evil and harm that it is harshness and severity, not mercy, is invalid. That is concerning the statement of two (principles). What is said to him?
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist belief in two eternal principles, light and darkness. Maturidi begins by arguing that if ignorance and incapacity are present in light, then the very reason for postulating a second principle (to account for evil) is undermined. He then examines the relationship between light and darkness: if light is not hostile to darkness and even loves it, that is evil; if it is hostile, that hostility itself is evil in the witnessed world. He anticipates the dualist objection that such judgments apply only in the witnessed world due to afflictions, but counters that the same logic applies to their denial of wisdom in creation. He then presents a dilemma: either both good and evil come from light (which would make light evil), or evil comes from darkness and good from light (which would attribute lying and folly to light), or both come from darkness (which would mean darkness produces good as well). Finally, he considers whether light is affected by evil happening to its friends: if it is saddened, it is not pure joy; if it is not saddened, then its evil actions are not merciful. Thus the dualist position is internally inconsistent.

الجهل
الإساءة
الذنب
السفه
الكذب
الندامة
القسوة
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism could still be true if good and evil are separate.
Correction: The trilemma shows that no separation works without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's trilemma, if both good and evil come from darkness, what is the consequence?
A) Darkness is purely evil.
B) Darkness produces good as well.
C) Light is all-powerful.
D) Dualism is proven.

Key Takeaway
The trilemma shows that no possible arrangement of the origins of good and evil can rescue the dualist position from internal contradiction.

Maturidi's Refutation of Dualism: The Self-Undermining Premise
Maturidi argues dualists cannot trust their own reasoning due to their premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi turns the dualists' own explanation of error against them to refute their theology.

Core Idea
If perception can be corrupted by darkness, the dualists' own argument may be corrupted.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful dialectical argument against the dualists. Rather than rejecting their premise outright, he accepts it for the sake of argument and shows that it undermines their own position. This technique, called argument from internal consistency, is a hallmark of Maturidi's method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist position
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi presents the dualists' claim: in the witnessed world, the mixture of darkness in light causes errors in perception.
Dualists believe in two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil).
2
See Maturidi's acceptance
Notice that Maturidi does not deny their premise. He says 'قيل' (it is said) and then uses their own logic against them.
This is a dialectical move: accepting the opponent's premise for argument.
3
Grasp the reversal
Maturidi then asks: if afflictions of darkness distort perception, what prevents your own statement (that there are two principles) from being a product of that same distortion? Your claim might be folly and harshness, not wisdom and mercy.
The reversal relies on the dualists' own standard: any statement made under the influence of darkness is unreliable.
4
Reflect on the implication
Conclude that the dualist position is self-refuting: they cannot guarantee the truth of their own foundational claim because they cannot prove their reasoning is free from corruption.
This argument does not require a positive proof of tawhid; it only shows the inconsistency of the dualist position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعمت الثنوية في جميع ما عارضنا من اختلاف الأحوال وتضادها أن ذلك كذلك في الشاهد لشوائب الآفات من الظلمة في جوهر النور فيرى الشيء بغير صورته وبها يقع التواتر للعلم بالأشياء . قيل : فما يبعد أن يكون قولك كذا ليس بحكمة ولا رحمة بل هو سفه وقسوة إنما كان منك لما شابك من آفات الظلمة فمنعك أن ترى كل شيء يجوهره وصورته ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If the dualists claim, in all that we have objected to regarding the difference and opposition of states, that that is so in the witnessed world because of the admixtures of afflictions from darkness in the substance of light, so one sees a thing not in its form, and thereby occurs [؟] for knowledge of things. It is said: Then what prevents that your statement itself is not with wisdom nor mercy, but is folly and harshness, which came from you because of the afflictions of darkness that have mixed with you, preventing you from seeing everything in its substance and form? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage is an argument turning the dualists' own explanation against them. The dualists argue that in the witnessed world, the mixture of darkness in light causes things to be seen not as they truly are, leading to error. Maturidi replies: if that is true, then the dualists' own claim (that there are two principles) might itself be a product of that corruption, not a true perception. Their statement may be folly and harshness, not wisdom and mercy, because the darkness in them prevents them from seeing the truth. Thus the dualist position is self-refuting: they cannot guarantee the soundness of their own argument.

Key Terms
الثنوية
الشاهد
الآفات
جوهر
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Maturidi is arguing for general skepticism about knowledge.
Correction: This is a specific refutation using the dualists' own principle against them, not a denial of the possibility of certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi use the dualists' explanation of error against them?
A) He agrees that error is due to darkness and thus their argument is also true.
B) He argues that if perception can be corrupted by darkness, their own argument may be corrupted.
C) He claims that only light gives true knowledge.
D) He says darkness has no effect on reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the dualists cannot consistently claim that their own reasoning is immune to the corruption they attribute to all perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists assumed their own reasoning was pure while claiming darkness corrupts others' perception. In what area of your life do you apply a critical standard to others that you do not apply to yourself? Will you honestly examine that today?
?
If you admit that your own understanding can be tainted by inner darkness—like desires, pride, or ignorance—how should that affect the certainty with which you hold theological beliefs? Are you willing to question your own assumptions as Maturidi questioned the dualists?

Opponents' Unintentional Affirmation of Tawheed
Maturidi classifies opponents' views on tawheed: materialists and dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both materialists and dualists, despite their errors, admit a single origin or a single true deity, thus inadvertently reinforcing the doctrine of tawheed.

Core Idea
Even opponents of monotheism are forced to agree on a single source of all things.

Maturidi examines the views of two major groups of opponents: the materialists (ahl al-dahr) who posit a single primordial substance, and the dualists (al-thanawiyya) who affirm one wise deity. Their admissions, though flawed, point to the inescapable logic of tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how Maturidi presents the views of materialists and dualists as indirect affirmations of tawheed.
This passage is concise; take time with each phrase.
2
Understand the materialists' view
Maturidi says materialists, despite differences, agree on a single beginning or pre-eternal matter. Identify the key terms: are they saying the origin is one?
The materialists believe the universe emerged from one substance.
3
Understand the dualists' view
Dualists affirm one wise deity; they do not give the second principle the status of lordship. Ask: How does this still affirm tawheed?
They limit the evil principle, so only the good is divine.
4
Reflect on the overall point
Maturidi shows that even opponents cannot avoid acknowledging an ultimate unity. This strengthens the case for tawheed.
This is a polemical argument from consensus of opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في طرق التوحيد
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم القول بالتوحيد من طرق: هو أن قول أهل الدهر على اختلافهم اتفق على واحد بادئ، أو قدم طينة أو هيولى، وهو واحد حتى اعترضت فيه الأعراض، وتغيرت عن الحال الأولى. وقول الثنوية إن الحكيم الرحيم العليم واحد، وإن معنى الآخر ليس هو بمعنى الربوبية بل هو ضد معناه؛ إذ هو سفه كله وشر.
A question on the ways of tawheed. The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the statement of tawheed is from ways: that the statement of the materialists, despite their differences, agreed upon one beginning, or upon the pre-eternity of clay or hyle, and it is one until accidents intervened in it, and it changed from the first state. And the statement of the dualists is that the Wise, Merciful, All-Knowing is one, and that the meaning of the other is not the meaning of lordship but rather is the opposite of its meaning; for it is all foolishness and evil.
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage is a short classification of the views of opponents of monotheism (tawheed) regarding the ultimate origin. Maturidi explains that the materialists (ahl al-dahr), despite their various schools, all agree on a single primordial substance—either a beginning, or pre-eternal clay (tin) or hyle (hayula). This substance is initially one, but then accidents (a'rad) intervene and cause change. So they admit a single origin for all things. As for the dualists (al-thanawiyya), they affirm that the Wise, Merciful, All-Knowing is one (referring to the deity of light or good), and the other principle (the deity of darkness or evil) is not in the sense of lordship but rather the opposite—it is all ignorance and evil. Thus, even the dualists admit a single true god. This indirectly reinforces the doctrine of tawheed.

Key Terms
أهل الدهر
هيولى
الثنوية
ربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe in two equal gods.
Correction: Maturidi explains that dualists affirm one wise deity and consider the other principle as foolishness and evil, not as a lord.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do dualists affirm about the second principle?
A) It is a second god equal in power
B) It is not the meaning of lordship but rather foolishness and evil
C) It is the same as the first principle
D) It is the material substance of the world

Key Takeaway
Both materialists and dualists, despite their errors, admit an ultimate unity—either a single primordial substance or a single wise deity—thereby inadvertently testifying to tawheed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you still act as if there are multiple 'origins'—as if your success, happiness, or identity comes from more than one source? How does this lesson confront that practical polytheism?
?
The dualists acknowledged one true God but also believed in an evil principle. Where do you see yourself giving 'lordship' to something other than God—perhaps your fears, desires, or routines? What step will you take today to dethrone that false lord?

The Paths of Tawhid and the True Meaning of Oneness
Maturidi enumerates the groups that affirm tawhid in general and then defines the correct meaning of the One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all groups affirm tawhid in a general sense, but Maturidi explains the true meaning of divine oneness as one in essence, attributes, and transcendence.

Core Idea
True tawhid means absolute uniqueness in essence, attributes, and being above all comparison.

Maturidi begins by showing that even the most divergent groups—materialists, dualists, Jews, and Christians—all claim to affirm divine oneness in some way. However, their affirmations are incomplete or flawed. He then clarifies the proper understanding of tawhid: that God is one in His highness, one in essence without any like, and one in attributes with no partner.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's listing of groups
Read the passage where Maturidi presents the views of the Dahriyya, Dualists, and the people of religions (Jews, Christians). Notice how each group claims to affirm tawhid.
2
Identify the common thread
Look for the statement that 'despite their differences, they all agreed on the One.' This is the general affirmation of tawhid.
The agreement is only at the surface level.
3
Examine the definition of 'the One'
Focus on the phrase 'This is the meaning of the One: that He is one in His highness, one in essence, and one in attributes.' Maturidi is giving the correct understanding.
This definition excludes any likeness or partner.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this true meaning of tawhid contrasts with the views of the groups mentioned earlier. Why does Maturidi emphasize that nothing can be like God?
This sets the stage for the critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في طرق التوحيد

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم القول بالتوحيد من طرق : هو أن قول أهل الدهر على اختلافهم اتفق على واحد بادئ، أو قدم طينة أو هيولى، وهو واحد حتى اعترضت فيه الأعراض، وتغيرت عن الحال الأولى. وقول الثنوية إن الحكيم الرحيم العليم واحد، وإن معنى الآخر ليس هو بمعنى الربوبية بل هو ضد معناه؛ إذ هو سفه كله وشر. وأهل الأديان يثبتون القدم للواحد حتى قال قوم بتجسمه من بعد، وقوم إن له ابنا. فهم على اختلافهم أجمعوا على الواحد، ونحو ذلك أنه ليس بذى شبيه؛ إذ محال ذلك؛ إذ لم يكن غيره فهو على ذلك؛ إذ الوجه الذي فيه شبه وجود في غيره من الحدث، وذلك بعيد، وهذا معنى الواحد؛ إنه إذ هو واحد في علوه، وواحد الذات [محال] من أن يكون له في ذاته مثال؛ إذ ذلك يسقط التوحيد، وقد بيناه، وواحد الصفات يتعالى عن أن يشركه أحد في حقائق ما وصف به العلم والقدرة والتكوين، بل كل وصف من ذلك لغيره به بعد وشحال مماثلة الحديث القديم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أعطى جميع البشر ممن له نظر التوحيد في الجملة، ثم نقض كل فريق منهم ما أعطى في الجملة بالتفسير إلا فريق من أهل الإسلام لزموا ما أعطاهم الجميع. وذلك نحو من يقول من الدهرية بالبارى وقدم البارى فجعل معه جميع الأعيان في الأزل، وفي ذلك إبطال التوحيد. ومن يقول بالطينة والهيولى فيجعلها واحدا ثم أتلفه وجعل ما لا يحصى على الانتقال والفناء. ومن يقول من الثنوية بالواحد العليم فهو يذهب إلى أنه واحد الجنس، إذ يجعل جميع الخيرات أجزاء له، وذلك قول المنانية ونحوهم من الزنادقة والمجوس فأبطلوا معنى الواحد بالقول بالجسم؛ إذ هو اسم ما يكثر منه. واليهود حققوا له شبه الخلق فيكثر به العدد حتى بلغ قولهم إلى حد إمكان الولد. والنصارى يقولون بالواحد فى الكيان، والثلاثة فى القنومات، منفى عن كل
Question on the ways of tawhid. The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the statement of tawhid is via ways: that the statement of the Dahriyya, despite their differences, agreed on one originating [principle], or they posited an original clay or hyle, and it is one until accidents occurred in it and it changed from its first state. The statement of the Dualists: that the Wise, Merciful, Knowing is one, and that the meaning of the other is not the meaning of lordship but rather its opposite; for it is all foolishness and evil. The people of the religions affirm preeternity for the One, until some said He became embodied afterwards, and some said He has a son. So despite their differences, they all agreed on the One, and that He is not like anything; for that is impossible; since there is nothing other than Him, He is upon that; for the aspect in which likeness exists in another is a [mark] of contingency, and that is far [from Him]. This is the meaning of the One: that He is one in His highness, and one in essence – [impossible] that He should have an example in His essence; for that would nullify tawhid, and we have explained it. And one in attributes, exalted above having any partner in the realities of what He is described with of knowledge, power, and origination; rather, every attribute of that from others is far and [incompatible] with the likeness of the contingent to the preeternal. And there is no power except with God. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: All human beings who reflect on tawhid granted it in general, then each faction nullified what it granted in general by their interpretation, except a faction from among the People of Islam who adhered to what all granted. That is like one of the Dahriyya who affirms the Creator and the preeternity of the Creator, but then places with Him all entities in preeternity, and in that is nullification of tawhid. And one who affirms the clay and hyle, making it one, then destroys it and makes countless things subject to transition and annihilation. And one from the Dualists who affirms the one Knower, he goes to the view that He is one in genus, since he makes all goods parts of Him – that is the statement of the Manichaeans and similar Zindiqs and Magians – thus they nullified the meaning of the One by saying He is a body; for 'body' is a name for what has multiplicity. The Jews made for Him a likeness to creation, so multiplicity increases by it until their statement reached the point of the possibility of a son. The Christians say one in hypostasis, three in persons, negated from each...
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage is a classification and critique of various approaches to tawhid (divine oneness). Maturidi begins by stating that all people who reflect on tawhid affirm it in a general sense, but then each group contradicts that affirmation through their detailed interpretations (tafsir). He then enumerates the major groups: first, the Dahriyya (materialists) who posit a single originating principle but then add other entities alongside God in preeternity, nullifying tawhid. Second, those who posit a primordial substance (tina/hyle) as one, then make it subject to multiplicity and change, destroying true oneness. Third, the Dualists (Thanawiyya) who affirm one wise God but then make the other principle (evil) opposite to God, effectively denying His absolute oneness. Specifically, the Manichaeans and similar groups reduce God to a genus, making good part of Him, and by saying He is a body they imply multiplicity. Fourth, the Jews affirm God's oneness but then liken Him to creation, leading to anthropomorphism and even the possibility of a son. Fifth, the Christians affirm oneness in substance but three persons, which also undermines true oneness. Maturidi then explains the correct meaning of 'one': He is one in His highness, one in essence with no example, and one in attributes with no partner. He emphasizes that any likeness to the contingent is impossible for the Preeternal. The exception is the Muslims (ahl al-Islam) who consistently maintain the general affirmation of tawhid without contradiction. The passage thus serves as a comparative theological critique, showing how only the Islamic conception of tawhid is coherent.

Key Terms
التوحيد (tawhid)
الدهرية (Dahriyya)
الثنوية (Thanawiyya)
البارى (al-Bari)
الطينة (tina)
الهيولى (hayula)
القدم (qidam)
الجسم (jism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that all sects fully agree on tawhid in the same way, but Maturidi shows that their agreement is only a vague general affirmation; their detailed beliefs actually nullify true oneness.
Correction: True tawhid requires absolute uniqueness in essence and attributes, which these sects fail to uphold.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the correct meaning of 'the One'?
A) One in highness, essence, and attributes
B) One in number but multiple in attributes
C) One in origin only
D) One in name only

Key Takeaway
True tawhid requires affirming that Allah is absolutely unique in His essence, attributes, and transcendence, with no likeness or partner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is one in a way that no other being shares any aspect of His essence, attributes, or highness? Where in your life do you still compare Allah to created things, even subtly?
?
The Dahriyya and Dualists had a notion of one god but corrupted it. How can you ensure your own understanding of tawhid is not similarly corrupted by cultural or philosophical influences?
?
Maturidi insists that tawhid requires absolute uniqueness in attributes. Are there attributes of Allah that you secretly believe are shared by humans in a comparable way? How can you purge that from your heart?

How the Sects Contradict Their Own Affirmation of Tawhid
Maturidi details how each sect's interpretation nullifies its general affirmation of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each group's specific beliefs nullify their general affirmation of tawhid, and why only the Muslims consistently maintain true oneness.

Core Idea
Every group except the Muslims undermines tawhid through their detailed doctrines.

Maturidi now sharpens his critique: while all reflective people grant tawhid in general, each group's interpretation (tafsir) effectively nullifies that grant. He walks through the same groups—Dahriyya, those who posit primordial matter, Dualists, Jews, and Christians—showing exactly how their specific beliefs contradict the unity they claim to uphold. Only a faction from the People of Islam remained consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's opening statement
Read the line: 'All human beings who reflect on tawhid granted it in general, then each faction nullified what it granted by their interpretation, except a faction from among the People of Islam.' This is the thesis of the passage.
2
Examine the Dahriyya critique
Notice how the Dahriyya who affirm the Creator and His preeternity then place other entities alongside Him in preeternity, thus making partners.
3
Examine the Dualist critique
Notice how the Dualists who affirm one Knower then reduce Him to a genus by making good parts of Him, and by calling Him a body imply multiplicity.
4
Examine the Jewish and Christian critiques
Notice how the Jews liken God to creation, leading to anthropomorphism and the possibility of a son. The Christians affirm one in substance but three persons.
This directly violates the true meaning of the One.
5
Reflect on the exception
Consider why Maturidi says only a faction from the People of Islam adhered to the general affirmation. What makes Islamic tawhid consistent?
This links back to the definition of the One from the previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في طرق التوحيد

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم القول بالتوحيد من طرق : هو أن قول أهل الدهر على اختلافهم اتفق على واحد بادئ، أو قدم طينة أو هيولى، وهو واحد حتى اعترضت فيه الأعراض، وتغيرت عن الحال الأولى. وقول الثنوية إن الحكيم الرحيم العليم واحد، وإن معنى الآخر ليس هو بمعنى الربوبية بل هو ضد معناه؛ إذ هو سفه كله وشر. وأهل الأديان يثبتون القدم للواحد حتى قال قوم بتجسمه من بعد، وقوم إن له ابنا. فهم على اختلافهم أجمعوا على الواحد، ونحو ذلك أنه ليس بذى شبيه؛ إذ محال ذلك؛ إذ لم يكن غيره فهو على ذلك؛ إذ الوجه الذي فيه شبه وجود في غيره من الحدث، وذلك بعيد، وهذا معنى الواحد؛ إنه إذ هو واحد في علوه، وواحد الذات [محال] من أن يكون له في ذاته مثال؛ إذ ذلك يسقط التوحيد، وقد بيناه، وواحد الصفات يتعالى عن أن يشركه أحد في حقائق ما وصف به العلم والقدرة والتكوين، بل كل وصف من ذلك لغيره به بعد وشحال مماثلة الحديث القديم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أعطى جميع البشر ممن له نظر التوحيد في الجملة، ثم نقض كل فريق منهم ما أعطى في الجملة بالتفسير إلا فريق من أهل الإسلام لزموا ما أعطاهم الجميع. وذلك نحو من يقول من الدهرية بالبارى وقدم البارى فجعل معه جميع الأعيان في الأزل، وفي ذلك إبطال التوحيد. ومن يقول بالطينة والهيولى فيجعلها واحدا ثم أتلفه وجعل ما لا يحصى على الانتقال والفناء. ومن يقول من الثنوية بالواحد العليم فهو يذهب إلى أنه واحد الجنس، إذ يجعل جميع الخيرات أجزاء له، وذلك قول المنانية ونحوهم من الزنادقة والمجوس فأبطلوا معنى الواحد بالقول بالجسم؛ إذ هو اسم ما يكثر منه. واليهود حققوا له شبه الخلق فيكثر به العدد حتى بلغ قولهم إلى حد إمكان الولد. والنصارى يقولون بالواحد فى الكيان، والثلاثة فى القنومات، منفى عن كل
Question on the ways of tawhid. The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the statement of tawhid is via ways: that the statement of the Dahriyya, despite their differences, agreed on one originating [principle], or they posited an original clay or hyle, and it is one until accidents occurred in it and it changed from its first state. The statement of the Dualists: that the Wise, Merciful, Knowing is one, and that the meaning of the other is not the meaning of lordship but rather its opposite; for it is all foolishness and evil. The people of the religions affirm preeternity for the One, until some said He became embodied afterwards, and some said He has a son. So despite their differences, they all agreed on the One, and that He is not like anything; for that is impossible; since there is nothing other than Him, He is upon that; for the aspect in which likeness exists in another is a [mark] of contingency, and that is far [from Him]. This is the meaning of the One: that He is one in His highness, and one in essence – [impossible] that He should have an example in His essence; for that would nullify tawhid, and we have explained it. And one in attributes, exalted above having any partner in the realities of what He is described with of knowledge, power, and origination; rather, every attribute of that from others is far and [incompatible] with the likeness of the contingent to the preeternal. And there is no power except with God. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: All human beings who reflect on tawhid granted it in general, then each faction nullified what it granted in general by their interpretation, except a faction from among the People of Islam who adhered to what all granted. That is like one of the Dahriyya who affirms the Creator and the preeternity of the Creator, but then places with Him all entities in preeternity, and in that is nullification of tawhid. And one who affirms the clay and hyle, making it one, then destroys it and makes countless things subject to transition and annihilation. And one from the Dualists who affirms the one Knower, he goes to the view that He is one in genus, since he makes all goods parts of Him – that is the statement of the Manichaeans and similar Zindiqs and Magians – thus they nullified the meaning of the One by saying He is a body; for 'body' is a name for what has multiplicity. The Jews made for Him a likeness to creation, so multiplicity increases by it until their statement reached the point of the possibility of a son. The Christians say one in hypostasis, three in persons, negated from each...
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage is a classification and critique of various approaches to tawhid (divine oneness). Maturidi begins by stating that all people who reflect on tawhid affirm it in a general sense, but then each group contradicts that affirmation through their detailed interpretations (tafsir). He then enumerates the major groups: first, the Dahriyya (materialists) who posit a single originating principle but then add other entities alongside God in preeternity, nullifying tawhid. Second, those who posit a primordial substance (tina/hyle) as one, then make it subject to multiplicity and change, destroying true oneness. Third, the Dualists (Thanawiyya) who affirm one wise God but then make the other principle (evil) opposite to God, effectively denying His absolute oneness. Specifically, the Manichaeans and similar groups reduce God to a genus, making good part of Him, and by saying He is a body they imply multiplicity. Fourth, the Jews affirm God's oneness but then liken Him to creation, leading to anthropomorphism and even the possibility of a son. Fifth, the Christians affirm oneness in substance but three persons, which also undermines true oneness. Maturidi then explains the correct meaning of 'one': He is one in His highness, one in essence with no example, and one in attributes with no partner. He emphasizes that any likeness to the contingent is impossible for the Preeternal. The exception is the Muslims (ahl al-Islam) who consistently maintain the general affirmation of tawhid without contradiction. The passage thus serves as a comparative theological critique, showing how only the Islamic conception of tawhid is coherent.

Key Terms
التوحيد (tawhid)
الدهرية (Dahriyya)
الثنوية (Thanawiyya)
البارى (al-Bari)
الطينة (tina)
الهيولى (hayula)
المنانية (Manichaeans)
الزنادقة (Zindiqs)
المجوس (Magians)
اليهود (Jews)
النصارى (Christians)
الجسم (jism)
القنومات (aqanim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Jews and Christians have a pure monotheism equal to Islam. Maturidi shows that their doctrines of anthropomorphism (Jews) and trinity (Christians) effectively nullify the oneness they claim.
Correction: True tawhid requires denying all likeness to creation and affirming absolute unity without multiplicity in person or nature.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which group is the exception that consistently maintains the general affirmation of tawhid?
A) The Dahriyya
B) The Dualists
C) A faction from the People of Islam
D) The Christians

Key Takeaway
Only the Islamic understanding of tawhid remains consistent by rejecting any form of multiplicity, corporeality, or likeness in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself mentally picturing God in a way that resembles a human or some created being? How does that image contradict the tawhid you claim to believe? Commit today to replacing every such image with what Allah has revealed about Himself, even if it feels abstract.
?
The Jews' likening led them to the brink of saying God could have a son. Are there any small 'likenesses' you tolerate in your own thoughts about God—like assuming He feels emotions exactly as humans do, or that He is bound by human senses of justice? Identify one and resolve to actively reject it using the negation tools of tawhid.
?
The Christians affirm one hypostasis but three persons—a division in the very being of God. Reflect: In your daily life, do you ever separate Allah's attributes as if they conflict (e.g., mercy vs. justice, will vs. power)? How can you worship a God whose attributes are perfectly united without division? Write down one practical step to harmonize your understanding.

Maturidi's Critique of Dualist Conceptions of God
Maturidi refutes dualists who make God one in genus by attributing all good to Him as parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualist claims that God is one in genus and why equating God with a body undermines the true meaning of the One.

Core Idea
Dualists make God one in genus by attributing all good to Him as parts.

Maturidi here addresses a subtle theological error: dualists like the Manichaeans claim to believe in one God but then describe Him in ways that compromise His absolute oneness. By making all good things 'parts' of God, they reduce Him to a genus. This lesson examines Maturidi's concise refutation and its implications for understanding tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualist claim
Read the passage to see what dualists affirm and how they describe God. They say He is the One, the Knowing, but then make all good things parts of Him.
Notice that they start with correct terminology but then err in their description.
2
Understand the error of 'one in genus'
Making good things parts of God turns Him into a genus—a category of beings that share an essence—rather than being absolutely unique. This diminishes His transcendence.
3
See how 'body' nullifies the meaning of the One
By calling God a body, dualists imply He can be multiplied and divided, which contradicts the true meaning of al-wāḥid (the One). Maturidi explains that a body is something that multiplies from itself.
This is the key point: a body cannot be truly one in the absolute sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن يقول من الثنوية بالواحد العليم فهو يذهب إلى أنه واحد الجنس، إذ يجعل جميع الخيرات أجزاء له، وذلك قول المنانية ونحوهم من الزنادقة والمجوس فأبطلوا معنى الواحد بالقول بالجسم؛ إذ هو اسم ما يكثر منه.
And he who says among the dualists of the One, the Knowing, he goes to that He is one in genus, for he makes all good things parts of Him, and that is the saying of the Manichaeans and the like of them from the heretics and the Magians, so they nullified the meaning of the One by saying of the body; for it is a name of that which multiplies from it.
Source pages p. 718

Commentary
This passage critiques the dualists (al-thunawiyya), including the Manichaeans (al-manāniyya), heretics (al-zanādiqa), and Magians (al-majūs). These groups affirm a single, knowing deity but then claim that all good things are parts of Him, effectively making Him one in genus rather than unique in essence. Maturidi argues that by describing God as a body (al-jism), they nullify the true meaning of al-wāḥid, because a body is something that can be multiplied and divided, which contradicts absolute oneness.

Key Terms
الثنوية
الواحد العليم
الجنس
المنانية
الزنادقة
المجوس
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dualists are entirely separate from monotheism, but they actually affirm a single creator while erring in describing Him.
Correction: Dualists claim to believe in one God but reduce Him to a genus and call Him a body, which compromises true oneness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how do the dualists nullify the meaning of the One?
A) By denying His existence
B) By making good things parts of Him
C) By claiming He has a son
D) By affirming prime matter

Key Takeaway
Dualists affirm a single deity but reduce Him to a genus by making good things His parts, and equating God with a body contradicts the true meaning of al-wāḥid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience good things in life, do you ever unconsciously treat them as 'parts' of God, as if His goodness is divided into pieces? How would your worship change if you saw every blessing as a direct, indivisible manifestation of His single, infinite goodness?
?
This passage warns against describing God in ways that limit Him. Have you ever caught yourself imagining God's attributes like a list of separate qualities, almost like parts of a whole? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's knowledge, power, and mercy are not distinct pieces but one undivided reality?
?
The dualists' error began with a good intention—to affirm God's unity—but they fell into misdescription. Reflect on your own attempts to understand God: are there ways you might be unintentionally confining Him to human categories (like 'father', 'king', 'light')? What step can you take today to let God be greater than any label you use?

The Jewish Conception of God: Likeness and Offspring
Maturidi critiques the Jewish conception of God by showing how likeness leads to offspring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Jewish anthropomorphic tendency that leads to the possibility of God having offspring.

Core Idea
Affirming likeness to creation opens the door to unthinkable consequences.

Maturidi begins by addressing the Jewish conception of God. He argues that by affirming a likeness (shabah) between God and creation, the Jews open the door to numerical multiplicity and even to the possibility of God having offspring. This is a serious violation of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Jews
Read the first part of the Arabic passage focusing on the Jews (until 'الولد').
Notice the chain: likeness → number → offspring.
2
Understand the logic
Maturidi argues that affirming any similarity leads to multiplying attributes and eventually to offspring.
Why is offspring impossible for God?
3
Reflect on tawhid
Consider how this critique upholds God's uniqueness and transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليهود حققوا له شبه الخلق فيكثر به العدد حتى بلغ قولهم إلى حد إمكان الولد. والنصارى يقولون بالواحد فى الكيان، والثلاثة فى القنومات، منفى عن كل قنوم الجزء والحد، ويقولون: كان غير مجسم ثم تجسم، ومعلوم أن الجسم هو صورة تتجزأ وتتبعض.
And the Jews affirmed for Him a likeness to creation, so that number multiplies by it until their saying reached the extent of possibility of offspring. And the Christians say of the One in entity and the three in hypostases, negated from each hypostasis part and limit, and they say: He was non-embodied then became embodied, and it is known that the body is an image that divides and partitions.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage critiques both Jews and Christians. The Jews, according to Maturidi, affirmed a likeness (shabah) between God and creation, which leads to numerical multiplicity and even the possibility of God having offspring. The Christians affirm one entity (kīyān) but three hypostases (aqnūm), claiming each hypostasis has no part or limit, yet they also say God was non-embodied then became embodied in Jesus. Maturidi points out that a body is inherently divisible and particulate, which contradicts true tawhid.

Key Terms
اليهود
شبه الخلق
الولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does affirming a likeness (shabah) between God and creation lead to?
A) Unity of God
B) Multiplicity and possibility of offspring
C) Incarnation
D) Trinity

Key Takeaway
Affirming any likeness between God and creation undermines tawhid and can lead to absurd conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining God with human-like features (like a face, hands, or anger)? How does Maturidi's warning that such 'likeness' logically leads to offspring—something you would never affirm—challenge you to purge even subtle anthropomorphism from your mind? Write down one mental image you hold and commit to replacing it with 'Nothing is like Him.'
?
When you describe God to your children or friends, do you use language that implies similarity to creation (e.g., 'God sits on His throne' without clarifying transcendence)? The next time you speak about God, what specific phrase will you pair with 'without likeness' to ensure tanzih is preserved?
?
The passage shows a small step (likeness) leading to an absurd conclusion (offspring). In your own religious life, are there small compromises or assumptions you make that could, if followed logically, lead to a serious deviation from tawhid? Identify one such subtle belief and resolve to trace its consequences with the same rigor Maturidi applied.

The Christian Conception of God: Trinity and Incarnation
Maturidi critiques Christian Trinity and incarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Christian doctrines of Trinity and incarnation as contradictions to divine unity.

Core Idea
God cannot be both one entity and three hypostases, nor non-embodied then embodied.

Maturidi turns to the Christians, who affirm one entity (kīyān) but three hypostases (aqnūm), each without part or limit. He also points out their claim that God was non-embodied and then became embodied in Jesus. Maturidi demonstrates how these claims are internally inconsistent and violate the true oneness of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Christians
Read the second part of the Arabic passage about the Christians.
Focus on the terms الكيان and القنومات.
2
Analyze the Trinity claim
Maturidi says they say 'one in entity, three in hypostases'. Consider how this compromises oneness.
Each hypostasis is said to have no part or limit, yet they are distinct.
3
Analyze the incarnation claim
They say God was non-embodied then became embodied. Maturidi counters that a body is divisible and composite.
How can the immutable God change?
4
Synthesize
Understand why both doctrines fail to preserve tawhid.

Source Text

And the Jews affirmed for Him a likeness to creation, so that number multiplies by it until their saying reached the extent of possibility of offspring. And the Christians say of the One in entity and the three in hypostases, negated from each hypostasis part and limit, and they say: He was non-embodied then became embodied, and it is known that the body is an image that divides and partitions.

The Jews, according to Maturidi, affirmed a likeness (shabah) between God and creation, which leads to numerical multiplicity and even the possibility of God having offspring. The Christians affirm one entity (kīyān) but three hypostases (aqnūm), claiming each hypostasis has no part or limit, yet they also say God was non-embodied then became embodied in Jesus. Maturidi points out that a body is inherently divisible and particulate, which contradicts true tawhid.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah is one, do you inadvertently think of His attributes as separate 'parts' or 'persons' in your mind? How does Maturidi's critique of the Trinity expose any hidden tendency in your heart to divide the indivisible? What will you actively do to train your mind to see Allah's attributes as a perfect whole without composition?
?
If you were to imagine Allah's mercy as something that increases when you obey and decreases when you sin, would that not be a form of believing He changes? The Christians said God changed from non-embodied to embodied. Do you ever act as if Allah's essence changes based on your actions? How does the lesson's definition of body as that which divides and partitions confront that assumption? What specific step will you take to root out any subtle belief in God's mutability from your heart?
?
The Christians negated part and limit from the hypostases yet still claimed they were distinct. Are you guilty of a similar contradiction in your own understanding of Allah's names? For example, do you think of 'Ar-Rahman' and 'Ar-Raheem' as somehow separate entities rather than aspects of one simple Being? What concrete practice can you adopt to unify your worship and avoid mental shirk?

Critique of the Followers of Natures
Maturidi refutes those who posit eternal natures that operate independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the belief in eternal natures that operate independently, and why this undermines tawhid.

Core Idea
Attributing pre-eternity to natural processes is a subtle form of shirk.

Maturidi's critique of the followers of natures targets a subtle form of shirk: attributing pre-eternity to natural processes. In this brief but dense passage, he exposes the logical inconsistency of their position. We'll examine his reasoning and its implications for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: الطبائع (natures) and أزلى (pre-eternal).
2
Understand the claim being refuted
The followers of natures hold that natures do not act by themselves; rather, there must be a combiner and separator. Yet they consider this process of combination and separation to be pre-eternal. Grasp this twofold claim.
3
Analyze Maturidi's critique
Maturidi points out the inconsistency: if the process of combining and separating is pre-eternal, then something other than God is eternal. This contradicts tawhid.
Reflect on how this refutes any philosophy that posits eternal inherent forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصحاب الطبائع لم يوجبوا الطبائع لأنفسها تعمل حتى يكون من يجمع بينها ويفرق، وذلك أزلى عندهم.
And the followers of natures did not make the natures obligatory for themselves to act, so that there is one who combines them and separates, and that is pre-eternal according to them.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This short passage critiques those who believe in eternal natures (al-ṭabā'i'). Maturidi says they do not consider the natures as necessarily acting by themselves, so that there must be an external agent to combine and separate them. Yet they consider this process of combination and separation to be pre-eternal (azalī), thus attributing pre-eternity to something other than God and undermining tawhid.

Key Terms
الطبائع
أزلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this passage is about the ancient Greek theory of four elements (earth, air, fire, water) as eternal natures.
Correction: While the term 'natures' might evoke Greek philosophy, Maturidi is refuting any belief system that posits eternal inherent natures operating independently, including certain Islamic theological sects. The critique applies broadly to any attribution of pre-eternity to natural processes.

Checkpoint
What do the followers of natures consider to be pre-eternal?
A) The natures themselves
B) The agent that combines and separates
C) The process of combining and separating
D) The creator

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the followers of natures by showing that their belief in a pre-eternal process of combination and separation attributes eternity to something other than God, violating tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that some natural processes or 'laws' are independent of God's direct control? How does this passage challenge you to examine your own assumptions about the world's workings?
?
The followers of natures acknowledged an external agent but still fell into error by attributing pre-eternity to the process. Can you identify any areas in your life where you might be acknowledging God's existence but still implicitly granting eternity or autonomy to something else?
?
Maturidi's critique reveals a subtle form of shirk. Are there any modern-day beliefs or systems (like materialism, 'the universe just works this way') that you might be unknowingly imbuing with a sense of pre-eternity or independence from God? How will you actively guard against this in your daily thoughts?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila's View on Pre-Eternity
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila for positing eternal things and compares them to materialists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that things exist in pre-eternity and why he compares their error to that of the Dahrīs and dualists.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's belief in eternal things alongside God undermines tawhid.

Maturidi here takes on the Mu'tazila, who claim to uphold tawhid but fall into the same trap as the Dahrīs by positing pre-eternal entities. Their error not only contradicts tawhid but also leads to absurd consequences that even the dualists and materialists avoid. In this passage, he exposes the incoherence of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claim
Carefully read the opening sentence where Maturidi states the Mu'tazila say things are in pre-eternity.
Note the terms al-qidam and al-azal.
2
Identify the comparisons
Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila to three groups: Dahrīs, dualists, and followers of hylē. Find each comparison.
The dualists believed in two eternal principles that mixed.
3
Understand the logical point
Maturidi says the earlier groups' errors are more logically binding than the Mu'tazila's. Why? Because they attribute change to events in God's essence, while Mu'tazila attribute change to events external to Him.
This is a subtle but important point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن منتحلى التوحيد المعتزلة، يقولون بالأشياء في القدم، واسم القدم يأخذ الأزل، فمثله الأشياء، فيبطل على قولهم التوحيد على ما بينا من قول الدهرية في قدم العالم، مع عند قيم غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم ثم رحيم ثم صار كذلك يحدث الأشياء على ما قالت الثنوية من التباين بالذات ثم الامتزاج، وعلى ما قال أصحاب الهيولى والطينة إنه كان واحدا على جهة ثم صار على تلك الحال حدث من الحوادث . لكن قول أولئك ألزم بحق العقل من قول المعتزلة؛ إذ هم ألزموا التغير بحوادث في الأصل، وهؤلاء بحوادث في غيرها، ولا أحد يتغير في الشاهد عما عليه بما لا يحل به، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى هذا القول " الحسين والبرغوث" وغيرهما في هذا الثاني، وهؤلاء أيضا الزموا التغير بالمكان حيث قالوا: كان ولا مكان، ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان، فألزموا الوصف بالمحدث، فيبطل معنى التوحيد.
And from those who claim tawhid are the Mu'tazila; they say of things in pre-eternity, and the name of pre-eternity takes the pre-eternal, so he likens things to it, so by their saying tawhid is nullified, as we explained from the saying of the Dahrīs about the pre-eternity of the world, with [unclear] not a creator nor a Merciful nor a Compassionate then Merciful then became thus and creates things as the dualists said of differentiation by essence then mixing, and as the followers of hylē and clay said that He was one in a manner then became upon that state and an event of events occurred. But the saying of those is more binding to the right of reason than the saying of the Mu'tazila; for they imposed change by events in the origin, and these by events in other than it, and no one changes in the witnessed from what he is upon by what does not inhere in him, and there is no power but in Allah. And upon this saying are al-Ḥusayn and al-Burghūth and others in this second, and these also imposed change by place when they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place, so they imposed description by the contingent, so the meaning of tawhid is nullified.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila and two later figures, al-Ḥusayn al-Najjār and al-Burghūth. Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila, despite claiming tawhid, posit that things (al-ashyā') are in pre-eternity (fī al-qidam), thus likening them to God and nullifying tawhid in a manner similar to the Dahrīs. He also compares them to dualists and hylē believers who posit change. He then states that the earlier groups' errors are more logically consistent than the Mu'tazila's, because the Mu'tazila attribute change to God through events occurring in Him, while others attribute change through external events. Then he critiques al-Ḥusayn and al-Burghūth for saying God was without place then later is described by every place, which implies a change in God and thus nullifies tawhid.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
القدم
الأزل
الدهرية
الثنوية
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila with atheists.
Correction: He compares their logical structure but still distinguishes them as claimants of tawhid.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the earlier groups' errors are more logically consistent than the Mu'tazila's?
A) Because they attribute change to events in God's essence
B) Because they deny tawhid outright
C) Because they are not Muslims
D) Because they believe in one eternal principle

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's belief in eternal things contradicts tawhid and is less rational than even the errors of dualists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat anything in your life—like a habit, a relationship, or even your own existence—as if it were 'always there,' permanent beside Allah? How does the Mu‘tazila’s error of attributing pre-eternity (qidam) to other things expose a subtle shirk in your own heart? Commit today to recite 'Huwa al-Awwal wa al-Ākhir' and consciously deny permanence to anything but Him.
?
Māturīdī says the error of the dualists is 'more binding to reason' than the Mu‘tazila’s. Are you ever more comfortable defending a less obvious error (like the Mu‘tazila’s) because it sounds more 'reasonable' than obvious shirk? Where in your life do you rationalize a compromised belief rather than fully submitting? Ask Allah for sincerity in thought and action.
?
The passage ends with 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā bi-llāh'—no power except through Allah. In what area of your life are you relying on your own strength or the perceived 'permanence' of created means? How does this verse dismantle that reliance? Make it your dhikr today.

Refutation of al-Husayn and al-Burghūth on God and Place
Maturidi refutes al-Husayn and al-Burghūth for attributing change to God through place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi refutes al-Husayn and al-Burghūth's view that God was without place then became described by every place, and how this implies change in God.

Core Idea
Saying God was without place and then is described by all places implies a change in God, nullifying tawhid.

Al-Husayn al-Najjār and al-Burghūth attempted to affirm God's transcendence by saying He was without place, but then claimed He is described by every place, inadvertently implying a transition. Maturidi shows how this reasoning leads to the very error they sought to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Read their statement
Read the words: 'كان ولا مكان، ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان' which means 'He was and no place, then He is described by every place.'
This implies a change from one state to another.
2
Understand Maturidi's objection
Maturidi argues that this imposes description by the contingent (al-muḥdath), because change is a trait of contingent beings.
Thus tawhid is nullified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And from those who claim tawhid are the Mu'tazila; they say of things in pre-eternity, and the name of pre-eternity takes the pre-eternal, so he likens things to it, so by their saying tawhid is nullified, as we explained from the saying of the Dahrīs about the pre-eternity of the world, with [unclear] not a creator nor a Merciful nor a Compassionate then Merciful then became thus and creates things as the dualists said of differentiation by essence then mixing, and as the followers of hylē and clay said that He was one in a manner then became upon that state and an event of events occurred. This passage critiques the Mu'tazila and two later figures, al-Ḥusayn al-Najjār and al-Burghūth. Misconception: Al-Husayn and al-Burghūth were earlier theologians (like the Mu'tazila).
Correction: They were successors of the Mu'tazila, as Maturidi critiques them after the Mu'tazila.

Maturidi's Refutation of the Anthropomorphists
Maturidi refutes the Mushabbiha who assert God has a likeness to creation in body and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific attributes that the Mushabbiha incorrectly attribute to Allah and why this violates the principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely unlike any created thing — even in movement, stillness, limit, or form.

The Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) claimed that Allah has a likeness to His creation in certain bodily and attribute-related ways. Imam Maturidi here identifies their specific claims and firmly denies them. This brief but powerful passage encapsulates the essential error of likening Allah to contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage and get a sense of the sect's claims. Notice the five attributes they list.
2
List the five attributes
Identify the five specific attributes the Mushabbiha attribute to Allah: corporeality (jismiyya), limit (hadd), termination (nihaya), movements (harakat), and stillness (sukun).
These are all traits of contingent beings.
3
Analyze their logical error
Understand that these attributes are signs of contingency—they prove that the world is created. Applying them to Allah makes Him comparable to creation, which is a violation of tawhid.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion
Note Maturidi's final exaltation: 'exalted is Allah above that.' This teaches us to uphold Allah's transcendence absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشبهة يقولون له مثال فى الخلق فى الجسمية والحد والنهاية والحركات والسكون، يحققون له ما به عرف حدث العالم، ويجعلونه مثالا له، جل الله عن ذلك.
And the Mushabbiha say there is for Him a likeness in creation in corporeality, limit, termination, movements, and stillness; they affirm for Him that by which the contingency of the world is known, and they make Him a likeness to it, exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) who claim that God has a likeness (mithāl) to creation in terms of corporeality (al-jismiyya), limit (al-ḥadd), termination (al-nihāya), movements (al-ḥarakāt), and stillness (al-sukūn). They attribute to God the very characteristics that are used to recognize the contingency of the world. In other words, they make God comparable to contingent beings, which is a direct violation of tawhid. Maturidi ends with 'exalted is Allah above that.'

Key Terms
المشبهة
الجسمية
الحد
النهاية
الحركات
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mushabbiha only attribute a body to God, but they also attribute movement and stillness, which imply change.
Correction: Maturidi lists five attributes, including movement and stillness, showing the full scope of their error.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned by Maturidi as something the Mushabbiha attribute to God?
A) Corporeality
B) Limit
C) Eternity
D) Movements

Key Takeaway
Allah is absolutely transcendent and bears no likeness to creation in any attribute, including those that define created beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah’s 'Hand' or 'Face' from the Quran, do you find yourself picturing something like a human hand or face? How does this lesson challenge that inner image, and what will you do to purify your conception of Allah?
?
This passage lists five created attributes—corporeality, limit, termination, movement, stillness. Honestly, have you ever unconsciously thought of Allah as being 'somewhere' in the sky or as 'watching' in a way that implies spatial limitation? What step will you take today to train your heart to affirm absolute transcendence?
?
The Mushabbiha claimed to exalt Allah by attributing these attributes to Him, but they actually reduced Him to the level of creation. Are there ways you unknowingly 'reduce' Allah by expecting Him to act like a human, or by feeling disappointed when He doesn't meet your time-bound expectations? Confess that to Him now and resolve to worship Him as He truly is.
