Refutation of Mu'tazila on Eternal Things
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of eternal things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim of eternal things alongside God contradicts the Islamic principle of tawhid.

Core Idea
Believing in eternal things beside God makes them partners in pre-eternity.

Maturidi begins by identifying a critical flaw in Mu'tazilite theology. Although they claim to uphold tawhid, their affirmation of eternal things alongside God actually undermines it. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the key terms: قدم, أزل.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila affirm that things are pre-eternal (qadīm) alongside God. Understand what 'pre-eternal' means in this context.
3
Understand the contradiction to tawhid
Maturidi argues that if things are pre-eternal, they share the attribute of pre-eternity with God, thus violating the uniqueness of God's eternal nature.
He compares this to the Dahriyya who believe in the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن منتحلى التوحيد المعتزلة ، يقولون بالأشياء في القدم ، واسم القدم يأخذ الأزل ، فمثله الأشياء ، فيبطل على قولهم التوحيد على ما بينا من قول الدهرية في قدم العالم، مع عند قيم غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم ثم رحيم ثم صار كذلك يحدث الأشياء على ما قالت الثنوية من التباين بالذات ثم الامتزاج ، وعلى ما قال أصحاب الهيولى والطينة إنه كان واحدا على جهة ثم صار على تلك الحال حدث من الحوادث . لكن قول أولئك ألزم بحق العقل من قول المعتزلة ؛ إذ هم ألزموا التغير بحوادث في الأصل، وهؤلاء بحوادث في غيرها ، ولا أحد يتغير في الشاهد عما عليه بما لا يحل به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى هذا القول " الحسين والبرغوث" وغيرهما في هذا الثاني ، وهؤلاء أيضا الزموا التغير بالمكان حيث قالوا : كان ولا مكان ، ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان ، فألزموا الوصف بالمحدث، فيبطل معنى التوحيد .
And among those who profess tawhid are the Mu'tazila: they affirm things in pre-eternity (qidam). The term 'qidam' includes azal (eternity without beginning), so the things become like Him. Thus their statement nullifies tawhid, as we have already explained regarding the statement of the Dahriyya about the pre-eternity of the world, while with a value is not a creator, not a merciful one, then merciful, then He became so and creates things according to what the Dualists said about distinction in essence then mixture, and according to what the proponents of hyle and clay said: He was one in a certain mode, then He became in that state, an accident occurred. But the statement of those is more binding to the truth of reason than the statement of the Mu'tazila; for they (the Dualists) necessitate change by accidents in the origin, but these (Mu'tazila) necessitate it by accidents in other than it. And no one is changed in the witnessed from his state by what does not inhere in him. And there is no power but with Allah. And upon this statement are al-Husayn and al-Barghuth and others in this second. And these also necessitate change by place when they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place. So they necessitate description by what is originated, thus the meaning of tawhid is nullified.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that there are eternal things alongside God. Maturidi argues that if things are eternal (qadīm), they share in the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) with God, which destroys the uniqueness of tawhid. He compares this to the Dahriyya (materialists) who assert the eternity of the world. He then points out that the Mu'tazila, by positing that God was not a creator or merciful and then became so, imply change in God, similar to the Dualists (Thunawiyya) who believe in two eternal principles that later mix. Similarly, the proponents of hyle and clay say God was one and then accidents occurred. Maturidi observes that though these earlier groups (Dualists, hyle theorists) also imply change, they at least locate change in the essence itself, whereas the Mu'tazila locate change in attributes like mercy or power. This is even more problematic because in the witnessed world, a thing does not change from its state due to something that does not inhere in it. He then mentions that al-Husayn al-Najjar and al-Barghuth held such views. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila who say God was without place and then is described by every place have necessitated a description with an originated attribute (al-muḥdath), thereby invalidating tawhid.

Key Terms
قدم
أزل
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila thought they were defending tawhid by denying eternal attributes, but their belief in eternal things outside God actually contradicts it.
Correction: True tawhid requires that nothing shares in God's attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). Affirming eternal things alongside God violates this.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what logical consequence follows from the Mu'tazilite claim that there are eternal things alongside God?
A) It leads to shirk by making those things partners with God.
B) It requires that God be a body.
C) It implies that God is not unique in pre-eternity.
D) It leads to the denial of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite affirmation of eternal things alongside God directly contradicts the essential uniqueness of God's pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart, treat anything in this world—your wealth, status, or even a relationship—as if it will never end, silently assigning it a kind of eternity that belongs only to God? What would it cost you to uproot that attachment and see it as truly passing?
?
If you truly believe that nothing besides God can be eternal, why do you still invest so much fear and anxiety in things that inevitably fade? How would your life change if your hope and trust rested entirely on the Eternal One alone?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending tawhid, yet their own claim made things partners with God in pre-eternity. Examine your own good deeds and acts of worship: is there any hidden belief that they are somehow self-sufficient or permanent on their own, independent of God's grace? Repent from that subtle partnership today.

Mu'tazila Imply Change in God
Maturidi argues that Mu'tazila imply change in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite position implies change in God, and why this is even more problematic than earlier heresies.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila must explain how God changes from non-creator to creator without inhering accidents.

Maturidi continues his refutation by showing that the Mu'tazila, in their attempts to explain God's actions, inadvertently attribute change to Him. This lesson examines the second major argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second part of the source passage. Pay attention to the comparison with Dualists and hyle theorists.
Look for terms like ثنوية, هيولى, طينة.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's implication of change
The Mu'tazila say God was not a creator or merciful and then became so, implying change in God.
This is a key point of their theology.
3
Compare with Dualists and hyle theorists
Earlier groups also implied change, but they located it in God's essence. The Mu'tazila locate it in attributes, which is worse because change from without is not possible.
Maturidi uses the principle: in the witnessed world, a thing does not change due to something not inhering in it.
4
Consider the place argument
The Mu'tazila also claim God was without place and then is described by every place, which implies change in God.
This again necessitates description with an originated attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among those who profess tawhid are the Mu'tazila: they affirm things in pre-eternity (qidam). The term 'qidam' includes azal (eternity without beginning), so the things become like Him. Thus their statement nullifies tawhid, as we have already explained regarding the statement of the Dahriyya about the pre-eternity of the world, while with a value is not a creator, not a merciful one, then merciful, then He became so and creates things according to what the Dualists said about distinction in essence then mixture, and according to what the proponents of hyle and clay said: He was one in a certain mode, then He became in that state, an accident occurred. But the statement of those is more binding to the truth of reason than the statement of the Mu'tazila; for they (the Dualists) necessitate change by accidents in the origin, but these (Mu'tazila) necessitate it by accidents in other than it. And no one is changed in the witnessed from his state by what does not inhere in him. And there is no power but with Allah. And upon this statement are al-Husayn and al-Barghuth and others in this second. And these also necessitate change by place when they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place. So they necessitate description by what is originated, thus the meaning of tawhid is nullified.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that there are eternal things alongside God. Maturidi argues that if things are eternal (qadīm), they share in the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) with God, which destroys the uniqueness of tawhid. He compares this to the Dahriyya (materialists) who assert the eternity of the world. He then points out that the Mu'tazila, by positing that God was not a creator or merciful and then became so, imply change in God, similar to the Dualists (Thunawiyya) who believe in two eternal principles that later mix. Similarly, the proponents of hyle and clay say God was one and then accidents occurred. Maturidi observes that though these earlier groups (Dualists, hyle theorists) also imply change, they at least locate change in the essence itself, whereas the Mu'tazila locate change in attributes like mercy or power. This is even more problematic because in the witnessed world, a thing does not change from its state due to something that does not inhere in it. He then mentions that al-Husayn al-Najjar and al-Barghuth held such views. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila who say God was without place and then is described by every place have necessitated a description with an originated attribute (al-muḥdath), thereby invalidating tawhid.

Key Terms
ثنوية
هيولى
طينة
بَرغوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes like mercy and creation are not essential, so He can acquire them without change.
Correction: If God acquires an attribute He didn't have, that implies change, which is impossible for God because it would require an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Mu'tazilite position on change worse than that of the Dualists?
A) The Dualists did not believe in God at all.
B) The Mu'tazila locate change in attributes, while Dualists locate it in essence.
C) The Mu'tazila deny the existence of accidents.
D) The Dualists are less known.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite theology, despite its intent to uphold tawhid, inadvertently implies change in God, which is a greater violation of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your obedience adds something to God or your sin diminishes Him? How does Maturidi's argument that God cannot change expose the arrogance of assuming we can affect His essence? Will you repent of treating God as if He is moved by your performance?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God was not merciful then became merciful. Are there areas in your life where you expect God to 'become' something He has always been—like expecting His mercy to increase after a good deed? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in His eternal attributes rather than your own actions?
?
Maturidi says change due to external causes would mean something outside God controls Him. When you pray, do you sometimes try to 'convince' God to change His will? How must your view of dua shift so that you align with His unchangeable decree rather than trying to alter it?

Refutation of the Anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha)
Maturidi refutes those who liken God to creation in body and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ascribing bodily attributes to God contradicts Islamic theology and leads to making God temporal.

Core Idea
Liken God to creation? You've made Him temporal.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi refutes the Mushabbiha, those who claim God is like created beings in body, limit, movement, etc. He exposes the internal contradiction in their reasoning: they use the same criteria that prove the world's temporality to describe God, which would make Him temporal. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the specific attributes mentioned.
The passage is concise; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the attributes ascribed to God
List the five attributes the Mushabbiha ascribe to God: corporeality, limit, finitude, movements, stillness.
These are all characteristics of created, temporal beings.
3
Understand the contradiction
Maturidi points out that the Mushabbiha 'confirm for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known.' Recognize that if God has these attributes, He would be temporal, which is absurd.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشبهة يقولون له مثال فى الخلق فى الجسمية والحد والنهاية والحركات والسكون ، يحققون له ما به عرف حدث العالم ، ويجعلونه مثالا له ، جل الله عن ذلك.
And the anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) say that He has an example in creation in corporeality, limit, finitude, movements, and stillness. They confirm for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known, and they make it an example for Him. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mushabbiha, those who liken God to created beings. Maturidi points out that they attribute to God the very characteristics—such as being a body, having a limit, an end, movements, and rest—that are used to prove that the world is originated (ḥādith). By doing so, they undermine their own argument for the temporality of the world, because they apply the same criteria to God. They make creation an example (mathal) for God, which is a gross anthropomorphism. Maturidi ends with 'jalla Allahu 'an dhalik' (Exalted is Allah above that), affirming God's transcendence.

Key Terms
تشبيه
مثال
جسمية
حد
نهاية
حركات
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to say God has a body as long as we do not think it is like human bodies.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any body-like attribute (limit, movement) entails temporality, which cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the Mushabbiha's claim according to Maturidi?
A) They deny God's attributes.
B) They ascribe to God the very properties that indicate temporality.
C) They say God has a son.
D) They claim God is pure light.

Key Takeaway
Ascribing bodily attributes to God compromises His transcendence and leads to the absurd conclusion that He is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, imagined God as having a form or being confined to a place? When did that happen, and what does this passage reveal about the danger of such thoughts? Will you actively correct your inner image of God starting today?

The Mushabbiha attributed limit and movement to God. In what subtle ways do you try to 'contain' God in your understanding, such as assuming He must act in ways that make sense to your limited mind? How does this passage challenge you to surrender to His absolute transcendence?
?
This lesson shows that even partial likeness to creation implies temporality. Are there areas in your life where you treat God as if He is like a human, expecting Him to conform to your expectations? What step will you take this week to increase your reverence for His incomparability?

The Three Divisions of the Dahriyya
Maturidi enumerates the three sects of the Dahriyya and their cosmogonies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct materialist cosmogonies within the Dahriyya as classified by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Materialists disagree even among themselves—Maturidi maps their chaos into three clear types.

Imam Maturidi does not simply dismiss the Dahriyya as a single group of atheists. Instead, he carefully distinguishes their internal disagreements, showing that they themselves cannot agree on how the world came to be. This passage presents a taxonomy of their views, which serves as a foundation for refuting them systematically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage contains several technical terms—take note of them.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three types of Dahriyya based on the order of separation (tabāyun) and combination (ijtimā‘). Find each one and note which sects are associated with each.
The first two divisions are explicitly named; the third is implied through the belief in eternal atoms with incipients.
3
Understand the significance of this classification
Reflect on why Maturidi starts with this taxonomy. How does knowing the internal differences of the Dahriyya help in refuting them?
This connects to the broader theological aim of demonstrating the necessity of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ربيع اختلاف الدهرية إلى ثلاثة ، إلى تباين ثم الاجتماع ، وذلك [قول] الزنادقة والثنوية ومن يقول بالنور والظلمة ، وإلى اجتماع ثم التباين ، وذلك قول من يقول بالطينة والهيولى مع [؟] الجهل بهما على القبول بالقدم ، ويشبه أن يكون هذا قول أصحاب الطبائع ، [على] أنه لم يظهر ذلك من قولهم ، ثم بقدم التفرق [ أو الاجتماع ويرون ما عليه العالم عليهما ، وعلى ذلك قول من يقول بقدم الأعيان مع حوادث لا أول ما .
Then the difference of the Dahriyya goes back to three [types]: to separation then combination - that is the saying of the Zandaqah and the Dualists and those who speak of light and darkness; and to combination then separation - that is the saying of those who speak of clay and hyle with [?] ignorance of them upon acceptance of pre-eternity, and it resembles that this is the saying of the proponents of natures, [although] that has not appeared from their saying; then to pre-eternity of separation [or combination] and they see what the world is upon as being upon them, and upon that is the saying of those who say the pre-eternity of individual atoms with incipients that have no beginning.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage classifies the Dahriyya (materialists who believe in the eternity of the world) into three main groups based on their views on the relationship between separation (tabāyun) and combination (ijtimā‘). The first group believes that things were originally separate and then came together; this is the view of the Zandaqah (heretics), the Dualists (al-thunawiyya), and those who posit light and darkness as eternal principles. The second group believes that things were originally combined and then separated; this is attributed to those who speak of prime matter (al-ṭīnah) and hyle (al-hayūlā) while being ignorant of them, accepting their pre-eternity, and it resembles the view of the proponents of natures (aṣḥāb al-ṭabā’i‘), though that is not explicitly stated. The third group believes in the pre-eternity of separation or combination and sees the current state of the world as being upon that; this includes those who say that individual atoms (a‘yān) are eternal along with incipients (ḥawādith) that have no beginning. The passage thus presents a comprehensive taxonomy of materialist cosmogonies, setting the stage for their refutation.

Key Terms
الدهرية
التباين
الاجتماع
الزنادقة
الثنوية
الطينة
الهيولى
الطبائع
الأعيان
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all Dahriyya are simply atheists with a single belief system.
Correction: Maturidi shows that they have distinct philosophical systems with different views on the relationship between separation and combination.

Checkpoint
How many divisions of the Dahriyya does Maturidi enumerate?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi identifies three distinct materialist cosmogonies among the Dahriyya, each with different views on the relationship between separation and combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an entire worldview as 'just atheism' without recognizing its internal diversity? How does Maturidi's classification challenge your intellectual humility?
?
When you encounter a belief system you reject, do you take the time to understand its internal logic as Maturidi does, or do you lump it into a single category? What would it cost you to do the former?
?
Consider the first division's belief in separation then combination: is there any part of your own life where you assume a similar pattern (chaos then order) without evidence? How might that assumption be mistaken?

Refutation of Mu'tazilite View on God's Power
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazilite claim about God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazilite restriction on God's power is self-contradictory and that their concept of 'possibility in the intellects' is baseless.

Core Idea
God's power is unlimited; Mu'tazilite restrictions are self-contradictory.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid directly confronts the Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot create actions as insignificant as a mosquito's. Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila themselves affirm God's power over everything, yet they inexplicably limit it. He then dismisses their foundational concept of 'possibility in the intellects' as meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Look for the Mu'tazilite claim and Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazilites claim God is able over everything, but then they restrict His power to 'most things' within the limit of possibility. Find this contradiction in the passage.
The phrase 'أكثر الأشياء' (most things) is key.
3
Analyze the dismissal of 'imkan fil-`uqul'
Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazilite concept of 'possibility in the intellects' as meaningless. Consider why this concept is problematic for the Mu'tazilite position.
This is a direct attack on their epistemological foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصاحب الكتاب ينتحل نحلة الإعتزال ، ومذهبهم أن الله لا يقدر على خلق فعل بعوض فما فوقه من الجواهر ، وفعل ذلك كله واقع تحت القدرة أو في ذلك لغيره قدرة ، فأبوا تحقيق ما ادعوا من أنه قادر على كل شيء في أكثر الأشياء التى هى فى حد الإمكان في العقول الإمكان في العقول لا معنى له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله في معارضة أمن المهم بالخارج عن حد
And the author of the book adopts the school of Mu'tazila, and their doctrine is that God is not able to create the action of a mosquito or what is above it of atoms, and all that action is under [His?] power or in that there is power for others. So they refused to realize what they claimed, that He is able over everything in most things that are within the limit of possibility in the intellects. 'Possibility in the intellects' has no meaning. And there is no power except with Allah. In opposition to the important matter of the external beyond the limit.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite position on divine power. It states that the author of the book (possibly referring to a specific Mu'tazilite work) holds that God cannot create the actions of a mosquito or smaller atoms, and such actions fall under some other power. The author refutes this by pointing out that the Mu'tazila claim God is able over everything, yet they restrict it to 'most things' that are within the limit of possibility in the intellects. He dismisses the concept of 'possibility in the intellects' as meaningless. The final phrase likely indicates that this restriction is in opposition to the obvious truth about God's unlimited power.

Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite position on God's power is reasonable and based on intellectual possibility.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is self-contradictory (claiming God is able over everything but then restricting it) and their concept of 'intellectual possibility' is meaningless.

Checkpoint

B) It has no meaning
C) It limits God's power properly
D) It is supported by reason

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot create tiny actions contradicts their own affirmation of God's power over all things, and their notion of 'possibility in the intellects' is baseless.

The Invalidity of 'Preservation without a Preserver'
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim using motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila claim of preservation without a preserving attribute is invalid due to the distinct nature of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are distinct accidents, proving that continuity requires a real attribute.

This lesson digs into Maturidi's refutation of a key Mu'tazila doctrine: that bodies can persist without an attribute of preservation. He argues that the very fact that motion and rest are different from each other and from the body shows that alternation between them requires a substratum with a preserving attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Maturidi begins by dismissing lengthy discussion and immediately states the core argument.
The key phrase is 'غيرية السكون والحركة' (the otherness of rest and motion).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila say 'ibqāʾ bilā baqāʾ' – preservation without a preserving attribute. What does this mean? Discuss.
Contrast with the Sunni view that Allah preserves bodies through an attribute of preservation.
3
Understand the proof
Maturidi says: the permissibility of each one (motion or rest) being a substitute for the other shows they are distinct from the body. This invalidates the Mu'tazila claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أطنب في هذا النوع فتركته لما لا منفعة فيه، وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر، وأنهما غيران ما يبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة في قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا، فزعم أنه لا يجوز؛ لما لا يثبت لكل شرط العدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك، وفي ذلك بطلان الوجود. واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار، مع في طير من يطير -- أن بينها ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية الأوليتها، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل. واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك، وقد ثبت فساد الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له، ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر، وفي ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا ما زعم.
Then he elaborated on this type, so I left it because there is no benefit in it. And among what he said of the proof of the otherness of rest and motion from the permissibility of each one being a substitute for the other, and that they are two other things which invalidates the statement of many of the Muʿtazila in their saying of 'preservation without a preserver' (ibqāʾ bilā baqāʾ). And there is no power except through Allah. Then he answered one who objected with: 'Thus the bodies are never devoid of what you mentioned.' So he claimed that this is not permissible; because it is not established that every condition of non-existence except by the existence of something else in that, and in that is the invalidation of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned of entering the house, along with regarding a bird that flies — that between them is a cubit from one side in a uniform flight, it is not possible that they are like that without an end to their priority, because the lifting of the end necessitates meeting in uniformity, and differentiation has occurred. And he argued by what is established of the contrariety of things like heaviness and lightness, heat and cold, and so on, and it has been established that a thing's corruption from a thing [proceeds] to what has no beginning, then from the nature of contraries is mutual repulsion, and in that is distance, especially when the proponents of this made what they claimed.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazila who claimed that bodies continue to exist without an attribute of 'preservation' (بقاء). The author first mentions that he skipped lengthy elaboration due to lack of benefit. He summarizes: the proof that rest and motion are distinct from each other and from the body invalidates the Muʿtazila's 'preservation without a preserver' (إبقاء بلا بقاء). Then he addresses an objection that bodies are never devoid of motion or rest, arguing that this would require an infinite regress of conditions leading to absurdity. He draws an analogy: two birds flying uniformly at a fixed distance apart cannot both be without a beginning (prioritizing one over the other). Finally, he uses the principle of contraries (تضاد) — like heavy/light, hot/cold — which naturally repel each other, implying that motion and rest are contrary accidents that alternate, and this requires a substratum, thus refuting the Muʿtazila position.

Key Terms
إبقاء
بقاء
سكون
حركة
غيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can continue to exist without any attribute from Allah.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even the alternation of attributes like motion and rest proves the body's need for a substratum and thus an attribute of preservation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use to refute the Mu'tazila claim of ibqāʾ bilā baqāʾ?
A) The eternity of the world.
B) The distinct nature of rest and motion.
C) The impossibility of a vacuum.
D) The oneness of God.

Key Takeaway
The alternation of motion and rest proves they are accidents distinct from the body, undermining the claim that a body can persist without a preserving attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever assume that your life simply continues by itself without any need for Allah's constant preservation? How does Maturidi's refutation challenge that assumption, and what commitment can you make today to acknowledge your complete dependence on Allah every moment?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could claim preservation without a preserver, but Maturidi exposed the contradiction. In your daily life, are there areas where you behave as if you can sustain something yourself without Allah's help? What practical step will you take to remind yourself that even the alternation of your rest and activity depends on His attribute of preservation?
?
Alternating between motion and rest, you experience change every day. Does this lesson make you more aware of the fragility of your existence? What specific thought or prayer will you incorporate into your routine to remember that only Allah's preservation keeps you in being?

Arguments from Infinity and Contraries
Maturidi presents arguments from infinite regress and contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments Maturidi uses: the bird analogy showing the impossibility of infinite regress, and the contraries argument proving the need for a substratum.

Core Idea
Infinite regress and contraries show that continuity requires a preserving attribute.

After establishing the basic refutation, Maturidi strengthens his case by addressing an objection: if bodies are never devoid of motion or rest, does that support the Mu'tazila? He shows this leads to absurdity. Then he uses the principle of contraries to demonstrate that opposing accidents cannot coexist, thus requiring a substratum that changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
An objector says bodies are always either in motion or rest. Maturidi argues this would require an infinite regress of conditions, which is impossible.
2
Analyze the bird analogy
Two birds flying uniformly at a constant distance apart cannot both be without a beginning; one must be prior. This illustrates the absurdity of infinite regress.
The analogy is about priority and end.
3
Understand the contraries argument
Contraries like heat and cold naturally repel each other. If a body is hot and then becomes cold, the contrary cold cannot simply appear without a substratum that accepts change. This shows the need for a preserving attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أطنب في هذا النوع فتركته لما لا منفعة فيه، وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر، وأنهما غيران ما يبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة في قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا، فزعم أنه لا يجوز؛ لما لا يثبت لكل شرط العدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك، وفي ذلك بطلان الوجود. واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار، مع في طير من يطير -- أن بينها ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية الأوليتها، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل. واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك، وقد ثبت فساد الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له، ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر، وفي ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا ما زعم.
Then he elaborated on this type, so I left it because there is no benefit in it. And among what he said of the proof of the otherness of rest and motion from the permissibility of each one being a substitute for the other, and that they are two other things which invalidates the statement of many of the Muʿtazila in their saying of 'preservation without a preserver' (ibqāʾ bilā baqāʾ). And there is no power except through Allah. Then he answered one who objected with: 'Thus the bodies are never devoid of what you mentioned.' So he claimed that this is not permissible; because it is not established that every condition of non-existence except by the existence of something else in that, and in that is the invalidation of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned of entering the house, along with regarding a bird that flies — that between them is a cubit from one side in a uniform flight, it is not possible that they are like that without an end to their priority, because the lifting of the end necessitates meeting in uniformity, and differentiation has occurred. And he argued by what is established of the contrariety of things like heaviness and lightness, heat and cold, and so on, and it has been established that a thing's corruption from a thing [proceeds] to what has no beginning, then from the nature of contraries is mutual repulsion, and in that is distance, especially when the proponents of this made what they claimed.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazila who claimed that bodies continue to exist without an attribute of 'preservation' (بقاء). The author first mentions that he skipped lengthy elaboration due to lack of benefit. He summarizes: the proof that rest and motion are distinct from each other and from the body invalidates the Muʿtazila's 'preservation without a preserver' (إبقاء بلا بقاء). Then he addresses an objection that bodies are never devoid of motion or rest, arguing that this would require an infinite regress of conditions leading to absurdity. He draws an analogy: two birds flying uniformly at a fixed distance apart cannot both be without a beginning (prioritizing one over the other). Finally, he uses the principle of contraries (تضاد) — like heavy/light, hot/cold — which naturally repel each other, implying that motion and rest are contrary accidents that alternate, and this requires a substratum, thus refuting the Muʿtazila position.

Key Terms
تضاد
نهاية
أولية
تنافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fact that bodies are always moving or resting proves they are self-sustaining.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to infinite regress and is absurd; the alternation of contraries proves the need for a substratum.

Checkpoint
What does the bird analogy illustrate?
A) Two birds can fly together forever.
B) Uniform flight without a beginning is impossible.
C) Birds need a creator.
D) Motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress and the nature of contraries both point to the necessity of a preserving attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that without a first cause, existence would be invalidated. Consider your own existence: where do you place your ultimate dependence? Is it truly on the First, or are you living as if you are self-sufficient? Write down one area of your life where you act as though you have no beginning and no need for a Preserver.
?
The argument from contraries shows that change requires a persisting core. Think of a recent change in your own character—a bad habit you tried to replace with a good one. Did you assume you could just swap without a stable self? What does that reveal about your need for Allah's ongoing preservation of your very essence? Commit to thanking Allah for that preservation today.
?
If the lifting of the end leads to uniformity, then your life without acknowledging a First Cause leads to moral and spiritual uniformity—no real differentiation between right and wrong. In what specific decision today are you about to act as if there is no first command from Allah, and thus no real distinction? How will you choose differently?

The Dahriyya Doctrine of Four Natures and Its Self-Refutation
Maturidi introduces the Dahriyya and their four-nature theory with initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core claims of the Dahriyya materialists regarding the four primary natures and how their own analogy of dyes undermines their position.

Core Idea
The Dahriyya's own example proves that mixture requires a mixer.

Imam Maturidi begins his critique of materialism by faithfully presenting the doctrines of the Dahriyya. He shows that even their own illustrative example—the mixing of dyes—implies an external agent, thereby inadvertently supporting the very tawhid they deny. In this lesson, we examine their theory of four natures and the fatal flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the Dahriyya's claims
Read the Arabic and translation. Note their agreement on the eternity of matter and their theory of four natures. Also note the analogy of dyes.
Pay attention to the use of 'طبائع' (natures) and 'امتزاج' (mixture).
2
Understand the analogy of colors
The Dahriyya compare the four natures to primary colors whose mixtures produce all shades without creating new colors. Reflect on why they think this supports their denial of a creator.
3
See Maturidi's initial refutation
Maturidi points out that dyes do not mix by themselves—they need an external mixer. Similarly, the four natures would need an external cause. This is the first step in refuting materialism.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the direction of argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم تذكر أقاويل الدهرية على ما ذكره ابن شبيـ وغيره ليظهر مذاهبهم فإن ظهورها أحد أدلة فسادها ، بعد أن يعلم اتفاقهم في قدم طينة العالم واختلافهم في قدم الصنعة وحدثها ، وهذا جملة مذاهبهم . أصحاب الطبائع أنهن أربع : حر / وبَرْد ، ونُدُوةٌ واختلف العالم باختلاف الامتزاج منها ، واعتدل ما اعتدل منها باستواء المزاج منها ، وعلى ذلك مجرى الشمس والقمر والنجوم ، ولم يزل يجرى" بمثل الذي يجرى كما ترى لا أول للأشياء ، وسموا حركاتها أعراضا ، وضربوا الباطلهم هذا مثلاً من نحو الأصباغ كالبياض والحسرة والسواد والخضرة ، إنها عند الامتزاج على قدر الكثرة والقلة والرقة والكثافة تختلف ألوانها لا أن يكون ثمة حادث لون ، وإن كان ربما يخرج على ما لا يعرف أهل هذه الألوان أن ذلك هم خرج ، فمثله ما ذكروا من الطبائع قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : فمن تأمل هذا القول بما ضربوا له من المثل وجده يثبت قول أهل التوحيد؛ لأن الأصباغ لأنفسها لا تمتزج ، ثم هي لو
Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: Then the opinions of the Dahriyya (materialists) are mentioned according to what Ibn Shabīb and others narrated, to make their doctrines manifest, for their manifestation is one of the proofs of their corruption, after it is known that they agree on the pre-eternity (qidam) of the matter (ṭīna) of the world and differ on the pre-eternity or temporality of the making (al-ṣanʿa). This is the sum of their doctrines. The proponents of the natures (al-ṭabāʾiʿ) say they are four: heat (ḥarr), cold (bard), moisture (nudūwa), and [dryness?]. And the world differs by the difference of mixture (imtizāj) from them, and what is balanced is balanced by the equalization of the mixture from them. Upon that runs the course of the sun, moon, and stars, and it has not ceased to run in the same way as you see, with no beginning for things. They called their movements accidents (aʿrāḍ). And they struck for this falsehood an example from colors like whiteness (bayāḍ), yellowness? (ḥasra), blackness (sawād), and greenness (khuḍra): that when mixed, according to quantity, scarcity, fineness, and density, their colors differ, not that a new color is generated. And if sometimes something occurs that those who know these colors do not recognize, it is as if it occurred. So similarly is what they mentioned of the natures. Shaykh Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: Whoever reflects on this statement with the example they gave will find that it confirms the statement of the people of tawḥīd, because the dyes (aṣbāgh) by themselves do not mix. Then if they…
Source pages p. 740

Commentary
This passage introduces the doctrines of the Dahriyya (materialists), who deny a creator and claim the world is eternal. Maturidi first notes that they agree on the eternity of the world's matter (ṭīna) but differ on whether the making (ṣanʿa) is eternal or temporal. He then presents the view of the 'proponents of the four natures' (aṣḥāb al-ṭabāʾiʿ): the four primary qualities are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness (though dryness is not explicitly mentioned here—the text has nudūwa and likely yubūsa omitted due to OCR). The diversity of the world arises from different mixtures of these four. When the mixture is balanced, the result is balanced. The regular motions of the sun, moon, and stars are eternal and have no beginning. They call these motions 'accidents' (aʿrāḍ). To illustrate their claim, they use the analogy of dyes (aṣbāgh): when mixed in varying proportions, colors appear—not as new colors generated, but as mere variations of the pre-existing colors. Similarly, they claim that the four natures merely mix without generating anything new. Maturidi then points out that this analogy actually supports tawḥīd, because the dyes themselves do not mix on their own—an external agent must mix them. The passage is cut off, but Maturidi is beginning to show that their own example refutes them: if the dyes require an external mixer, then the natures also require an external creator.

Key Terms
دهرية (Dahriyya)
طبائع (ṭabāʾiʿ)
امتزاج (imtizāj)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ)
صنعة (ṣanʿa)
طينة (ṭīna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Dahriyya's analogy of mixing dyes supports materialism by showing variation without generation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that dyes do not mix by themselves; an external agent is required, so the analogy actually supports theism.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the Dahriyya use to explain the diversity of the world without a creator?
A) The mixture of heat and cold in the body.
B) The mixing of dyes (colors) producing different shades without generating new colors.
C) The rotation of the sun and moon.
D) The change of seasons.

Key Takeaway
The Dahriyya's analogy of mixing dyes inadvertently proves the need for an external creator because mixtures do not occur spontaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, upon closer examination, contained its own refutation—just as the Dahriyya's analogy of dyes proved the opposite of what they intended? When you first encountered this passage, did you resist the truth it showed, or did you accept it immediately? Today, what is one assumption you cling to that might actually disprove itself if you looked at it honestly?
?
This lesson shows that even the manifest falsehood of a doctrine is a proof of its corruption. Think of your own life: are there areas where you continue to act on a false premise simply because you have not allowed yourself to see its absurdity? What would it cost you to bring that falsehood into the light of truth, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
The materialists' example of mixing dyes required an external agent to mix them. In the same way, your very existence and every 'mixture' of your abilities, circumstances, and choices demand an external cause—your Creator. Do you live as though you are the mixer of your life, or do you acknowledge that He is the One who sustains and orders it all? If He were to withdraw His action for an instant, what would become of you?

Refutation of Dualism: Logical Incoherence of Two Eternal Principles
Maturidi refutes dualism by logical necessity of separation and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument against dualism, which demonstrates that the concept of two eternal principles is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Two eternal principles cannot coexist without contradiction.

Maturidi addresses the dualist claim that there are two eternal principles, one good and one evil, one light and one darkness. He first describes the inevitable nature of such a supposed evil deity, then provides a rigorous logical proof that the very notion of two distinct eternal principles collapses into absurdity. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the author's description of the dualist god and the logical argument that follows.
2
Understand the description of the dualist god
Maturidi describes the second god as blind, powerless, weak, and evil by nature. Reflect on why this description is a reductio ad absurdum of the dualist view.
Dualists often claimed an evil principle; Maturidi highlights its deficiencies.
3
Follow the general principle of separation
Maturidi states that any two distinct entities must each occupy a separate place. This is the foundation of his argument.
4
Explore the two cases: body or accident
If the two principles are accidents, they are impermanent and cannot be eternal. If they are bodies, further analysis reveals contradictions.
Use the guided_breakdown and learning_flow to visualize the argument.
5
Conclude the refutation
Whether the two are bodies or accidents, the dualist position is untenable. Either they cannot coexist (light and darkness) or they mix good and evil, defeating the claim of separate principles.
Maturidi ends with a prayer, emphasizing reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاله عليه : أعمى لا يبصر ، عاجز لا يقدر ، ضعيف لا يقوى ، شر بالطبع لا بالقوة ، فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن العدول عن سبيله والابتلاء بشبكة الثنوية ، فإنه لا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما كل اثنين لا بد من انفراد كل بمكان إن كان جسما أو عرضا فإن كان عرضا فمفارقته توجب تافه ، وإن كان جسمها فإما أن يكون مكان كل واحد منهما من جوهره فلا يقوم فى مضادة حاله كالماني في البر والليلي من البصر بالنهار ، وإن كان من غير جوهره ألف الخير بالشر، والشر بالخير وذلك ينقض معتمدهم فى القول بالعدد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
He said concerning it: Blind does not see, powerless does not have power, weak does not have strength, evil by nature not by power. So we ask God to protect us from deviating from His path and from being afflicted by the net of dualism, for there is no power except with God. Moreover, every two must necessarily be separate each in a place, whether it is a body or an accident. If it is an accident, its separation necessitates impermanence. If it is a body, then either the place of each of them is from its substance, so it cannot stand in opposition to its state, like the one who prevents in the land and the nocturnal from seeing by day. Or if it is from other than its substance, good would mix with evil, and evil with good, and that undermines their reliance on the claim of duality. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 740

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist belief that there are two eternal principles. The author begins by describing the dualist conception of two gods: one blind, powerless, weak, and evil by nature. He seeks God's protection from such belief. Then he presents a logical argument: if there are two, they must each occupy a separate space, whether they are bodies or accidents. If they are accidents, they are impermanent and cannot be eternal. If they are bodies, either their location is inherent in their substance, causing contradictory states (like light and darkness) to be impossible simultaneously, or their location is not inherent, leading to a mixture of good and evil, which contradicts the dualist's claim of separate principles. Thus dualism is self-refuting.

Key Terms
ثنوية
جوهر
عرض
مضادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is refuted only by revelation or faith, not by reason.
Correction: Maturidi shows that dualism can be refuted purely through logical reasoning, using principles of space, substance, and contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if the two principles are bodies, what problem arises if their place is from their own substance?
A) They would be in different places, but then they couldn't interact.
B) They cannot stand in opposition to their state, like light and darkness.
C) They would mix good and evil.
D) They would be impermanent accidences.

Key Takeaway
Dualism is logically impossible because two eternal principles would necessarily conflict, either by opposition or by mixing good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing the evil in your life to a power independent of Allah, as if there is a separate force that can thwart His will? What does this lesson teach you about the logical necessity of Allah's sole sovereignty over all that happens?
?
When you face suffering, are you tempted to believe that evil has its own eternal existence, making you despair of Allah's complete control? How can the argument about the incoherence of dualism strengthen your tawhid in moments of hardship?
?
This passage shows that two eternal principles are logically impossible. In what areas of your life do you still act as if two forces are vying for control, rather than submitting to the oneness of Allah? What step will you take today to align your belief and action with absolute tawhid?

A Variant of the Eternal Natures Theory
Maturidi mentions a group who held a similar eternal-nature view but denied knowledge of the number of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claims of a group that held natures to be eternal but denied knowledge of the number of genera, as reported by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Some nature theorists claimed eternal natures with an unknowable number of genera.

Maturidi continues his survey of false beliefs by mentioning another group who shared the view of eternal natures but added that the number of genera of natures is unknown. This passage highlights the diversity within heretical views and sets the stage for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once and then its literal English translation. Notice the two claims the group makes.
The passage is short, so read carefully.
2
Identify the two key claims
Identify that this group claimed (1) there is no known number for the genera of natures, and (2) all things in their entirety, including winds and spatial directions, are eternal and connected to the natures.
These claims are distinct from the earlier group mentioned by Maturidi.
3
Compare with the previous group
Consider how this variant differs from the earlier nature theorists: both believe in eternal natures, but this group explicitly denies knowledge of the number of genera and extends eternity to all phenomena.
This shows internal diversity among heretical sects.
4
Recognize Maturidi's reporting style
Understand that Maturidi is reporting this view as a false belief, not endorsing it. He presents it to later refute it.
Always distinguish between the author's own view and the views he reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوم قالوا بمثل ذلك إلا أنهم زعموا أن ليس لأجناس الطبائع عدد يعرفونه، وكلهم قالوا بقدم الأشياء في جميع جملتها من مهب الشمال والجنوب والدبور والصبا ومن أعلاها وأسفلها متصلة به
And some people said something similar, except they claimed that there is no known number for the genera of natures, and all of them asserted the eternity of things in all their entirety, from the blowing of the north, south, west and east winds, and from their highest and lowest, connected to it.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This is a brief classification of an alternative view within the group of nature theorists. The author notes that another faction agreed with the basic idea (that natures are eternal and self-acting) but differed in two points: first, they denied knowing the exact number of genera (أجناس) of natures; second, they explicitly asserted the eternity of all things, including the winds and all spatial directions, claiming everything is perpetually connected to the natures. This shows that the theory of natures had multiple variants, but all shared the core error of denying a creator.

Key Terms
طبائع
أجناس
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is Maturidi's own view on natures.
Correction: Maturidi is reporting a heretical position, not endorsing it. He presents it as a false belief to be refuted.

D) The genera are created.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows a variant of the nature theory that claimed eternal natures with an unknown number of genera and included all phenomena in that eternity.

Exposition of Hyle and Aristotelian Categories
Maturidi presents the hyle doctrine and Aristotle's categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist doctrine of eternal hyle and Aristotle's ten categories as presented by Maturidi for refutation.

Core Idea
Matter was claimed eternal, and Aristotle's categories were said to exhaust all reality.

Maturidi presents a philosophical worldview that denies creation by attributing the world to eternal matter with an inherent power, and complements it with Aristotle's categorical framework. This sets the stage for his refutation of these false doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original state of hyle
Read the description of hyle as eternal, devoid of all accidents, and possessing an unconscious power. Notice the list of attributes it lacks.
This sets up the claim that matter is self-sufficient.
2
See how accidents arise from power
The passage explains that hyle's power, acting by nature (not choice), produces accidents, transforming hyle into substance (jawhar), the one substance of the world.
This is the mechanism for the world's formation without a creator.
3
Review Aristotle's ten categories
The passage lists the ten categories from Aristotle's Logic: substance, place, quality, time, quantity, relation, possession, action, agent, patient. Note the examples given for each.
These categories were claimed to cover all possible statements.
4
Recognize the claim of completeness
The passage asserts that nothing falls outside these ten categories. Reflect on why Maturidi includes this detail—it highlights the philosophical ambition to exhaust reality.
This claim will be critiqued in Maturidi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم طينة العالم كانت قديمة، سميت هيولى، معها قوة لم تزل بصفتها، ولا طول لها ولا عرض، ولا عمق ولا وزن ولا مساحة ولا لون ولا طعم ولا رائحة، ولا لين ولا خشونه ولا حر ولا برد ولا بلة، ولا حركة ولا سكون. ولا شيء معها في أوليتها من الأعراض، سميت إذ ذاك هيولى، وقلبت الهيولى القوة بطباع منها لا باختيار فحدثت هذه الأعراض، فسمى جوهرا، وهو جوهر واحد، وهو جوهر العالم، والافتراق والاتفاق إنما جاء من قبل الأعراض، والأعراض لا توصف بالاختلاف والاتفاق؛ لأنها لا يكونان إلا بغيرهما، والعرض لا يقوم بالعرض وإنما يقوم بالجوهر، فاختلف به الجوهر واتفق. وذكر أرسطاطاليس وهو صاحب هذا القول في كتابه الذي سماه المنطق عشرة أبواب: باب العين، كقولك إنسان، سميت عينه، وباب المكان، كقولك أين، والصفة بقولك كيف، والوقت متى، والعدد بكم، والمضاف مما في ذكر الواحد ذكر الآخر، كالأب والعبد والشريك ونحوه، وذو، كقولك: ذو شرف وذو أهل ونحو ذلك سموه باب الجدة، والنصبة كالقيام والقعود، والفاعل كقولك: أكل ونحوه، والمفعول كقولك: مأكول، لا يقدر أحد أن يذكر ما يخرج عن جملة ذلك. وزعموا في القوة إنها جاهلة تفعل بالطباع وليس بالهيولى حاجة إلى الأعراض.
And they claimed that the clay (substance) of the world was eternal, called hyle (hayula), with it a power never ceasing in its attribute, having no length nor width, no depth nor weight nor area nor color nor taste nor smell, no softness nor roughness, no heat nor cold nor wetness, no motion nor rest. And nothing with it in its primordium of accidents (a‘rad), it was then called hyle, and the hyle turned the power by natures from it without choice, so these accidents came to be, and it was called substance (jawhar), and it is one substance, and it is the substance of the world, and separation and union only came from accidents, and accidents are not described with difference and agreement; because they do not occur except by other than them, and an accident does not subsist in an accident but only subsists in substance, so the substance differs and agrees by it. And Aristotle, who is the proponent of this statement, mentioned in his book called Logic ten categories (abwab): the category of substance (al-‘ayn), like your saying 'man' – they called it its substance; the category of place (al-makan), like your saying 'where'; the category of quality (al-sifa), like your saying 'how'; the category of time (al-waqt), 'when'; the category of quantity (al-‘adad), 'how much'; the category of relation (al-mudaf), where mentioning one mentions the other, like father, slave, partner and the like; the category of possession (dhu), like your saying 'possessor of honor' and 'possessor of family' and the like – they called this the category of having (al-jidda); the category of action (al-nasba?), like standing and sitting; the category of agent (al-fa‘il), like your saying 'he ate' and the like; the category of patient (al-maf‘ul), like your saying 'eaten'. No one is able to mention anything that falls outside that total. And they claimed about the power that it is ignorant, acting by nature, and the hyle has no need for accidents.
Source pages p. 746

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a materialist doctrine held by some philosophers. It describes the theory of eternal matter (hyle) devoid of all accidents, which then, through an inherent power acting by nature, generates accidents and becomes the one substance of the world. The process of separation and union (formation of bodies) is explained through accidents. The passage then lists Aristotle's ten categories from his logic, which were used to categorize all possible statements. This presentation sets up the view that Maturidi intends to refute, as it attributes the world's existence to eternal unconscious matter and denies the need for a creator.

Maturidi presents the materialist and Aristotelian views as false systems that he will refute, emphasizing their claim to self-sufficiency and exhaustiveness.

?

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Eternal Matter: Hyle and Power
Maturidi presents the philosophers' doctrine of eternal hyle, its negative attributes, and the generation of accidents and substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that the world's matter is eternal, described negatively, and that an inherent power generates accidents to form substance.

Core Idea
The philosophers believed matter is eternal, without qualities, and accidents arise from an inherent power.

Before refuting the philosophers, Maturidi lays out their doctrine in detail. This passage describes the primordial state of the world's matter (hyle) as eternal and devoid of all attributes. An inherent power, acting by nature, then produces accidents, giving rise to substance. Understanding this sets the stage for the Islamic response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hyle
Read the Arabic text describing the hyle and its attributes. Note the long list of negations—no length, color, etc. This establishes the hyle's featureless state.
The negations emphasize that the hyle is pure potentiality.
2
Identify the role of 'power' (quwwa)
Find where the text says 'the power turned the hyle by its nature' and notice that the power acts without choice. This is a key point for later refutation.
The power is described as ignorant, acting by nature.
3
Understand the emergence of accidents and substance
Trace how accidents arise from the power's action, transforming the hyle into jawhar (substance). The text says this one jawhar is the substance of the entire world.
Separation and conjunction come from accidents, not from the substance itself.
4
Reflect on the purpose of this exposition
Ask yourself why Maturidi presents this view in detail. The answer: to refute it. Note that he does not endorse it but sets it up for critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم طينة العالم كانت قديمة، سميت هيولى، معها قوة لم تزل بصفتها، ولا طول لها ولا عرض، ولا عمق ولا وزن ولا مساحة ولا لون ولا طعم ولا رائحة، ولا لين ولا خشونة ولا حر ولا برد ولا بلة، ولا حركة ولا سكون. ولا شيء معها في أوليتها من الأعراض، سميت إذ ذاك هيولى، وقلبت الهيولى القوة بطباع منها لا باختيار فحدثت هذه الأعراض، فسمى جوهرا، وهو جوهر واحد، وهو جوهر العالم، والافتراق والاتفاق إنما جاء من قبل الأعراض، والأعراض لا توصف بالاختلاف والاتفاق؛ لأنها لا يكونان إلا بغيرهما، والعرض لا يقوم بالعرض وإنما يقوم بالجوهر، فاختلف به الجوهر واتفق. وذكر أرسطاطاليس وهو صاحب هذا القول في كتابه الذي سماه المنطق عشرة أبواب: باب العين، كقولك إنسان، سميت عينه، وباب المكان كقولك أين، والصفة بقولك كيف، والوقت متى، والعدد بـ "كم"، والمضاف مما في ذكر الواحد ذكر الآخر، كالأب والعبد والشريك ونحوه، وذو، كقولك: ذو شرف وذو أهل ونحو ذلك سموه باب الجدة، والنصبة كالقيام والقعود، والفاعل كقولك: أكل ونحوه، والمفعول كقولك: مأكول، لا يقدر أحد أن يذكر ما يخرج عن جملة ذلك. وزعموا في القوة إنها جاهلة تفعل بالطباع وليس بالهيولى حاجة إلى الأعراض.
And they claimed that the clay (ṭīna) of the world was eternal, named hyle (hayūlā), with it a power that never ceased in its attribute, having no length nor width, no depth nor weight, no area nor color nor taste nor smell, no softness nor hardness, no heat nor cold nor moisture, no motion nor stillness. And nothing with it in its primacy of accidents; it was then called hyle. And the power turned the hyle by its nature, not by choice, and these accidents occurred, so it was called jawhar (atom/substance), and it is one jawhar, and it is the jawhar of the world. Separation and conjunction came only from accidents, and accidents are not described by difference and conjunction because they only occur through other than themselves. The accident does not subsist in an accident but only subsists in the jawhar, so through it the jawhar differed and conjoined. And Aristotle, who is the proponent of this view, mentioned in his book which he called Logic ten categories: the category of substance (al-ʿayn), like saying 'human' – called its essence; the category of place (al-makān) like saying 'where'; attribute (al-ṣifa) like saying 'how'; time (al-waqt) like 'when'; quantity (al-ʿadad) by 'how much'; relation (al-muḍāf) whereby mentioning one mentions the other, like father, slave, partner and the like; and possession (dhū) like saying 'possessor of honor' and 'possessor of family' and the like – they call it the category of state (al-jidda); and posture (al-nuṣba) like standing and sitting; and action (al-fāʿil) like saying 'he ate' and the like; and passion (al-mafʿūl) like saying 'eaten' – no one can mention anything that falls outside that total. And they claimed concerning the power that it is ignorant, acting by nature, and the hyle has no need of accidents.
Source pages p. 746

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical doctrine that the material substratum of the world (ṭīna) is eternal, called hyle (hayūlā). The author describes its properties negatively: it has no dimensions, qualities, or accidents. An eternal power inherent in the hyle, acting by nature and not by choice, generates accidents, transforming the hyle into jawhar (atom/substance), which is the single substance of the world. All difference and combination in the world arise from accidents, which subsist only in jawhar. The passage then lists Aristotle's ten categories, which are said to exhaust all conceivable predicates. Finally, it notes the philosophers' claim that the power is ignorant and acts by nature, with the hyle needing no accidents. The author is setting up the view for refutation.

Key Terms
هيولى
قوة
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidi endorses the philosophers' view because he presents it without immediate criticism.
Correction: Maturidi is presenting the view for the purpose of refutation. He does not accept the eternity of the world or the existence of an inherent power acting by nature.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' view as presented by Maturidi, what is the initial state of the hyle?
A) It has length, width, and color.
B) It has no length, width, depth, color, taste, etc.
C) It has all qualities in potential.
D) It is a mixture of four elements.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' doctrine posits an eternal, quality-less matter (hyle) with an inherent power that generates accidents, leading to a single substance underlying all change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers believed that power acts by its nature, not by choice. Examine your own life: do you ever attribute the regularities of your day—sunrise, health, abilities—to 'nature' or 'habit' rather than to the constant, conscious will of Allah? Write down one moment today where you will deliberately remember that every accident (every event) is directly from Allah’s choice, not from the nature of things.
?
The hyle is described as completely without any qualities. Yet from it arises all diversity. Consider your own soul: do you sometimes believe that your 'natural self' (without divine guidance) can produce good deeds on its own? How does this passage remind you that every good accident (thought, intention, action) must be given by Allah, not produced by your own inherent power?
?
The philosophers claim that the world’s substance is one and eternal. This directly contradicts the Islamic belief that Allah alone is eternal and that He created the world from nothing. Pause and ask yourself: Is there any area of your life where you act as if something other than Allah is eternal or self-sufficient—like money, health, relationships? Commit to acknowledging Allah’s sole eternity in that area today.

Aristotle's Ten Categories as Presented by Maturidi
Maturidi lists Aristotle's ten categories with examples and asserts their exhaustiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and define the ten categories Aristotle proposed, as transmitted by Maturidi, and understand their claim to exhaust all predicates.

Core Idea
Aristotle's ten categories claim to cover every possible predicate about anything.

After presenting the philosophers' doctrine of matter, Maturidi introduces Aristotle's ten categories, which are said to encompass all that can be said about anything. This list is a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic and metaphysics. Maturidi presents it as part of the philosophical worldview he will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic list of categories
Read the Arabic text from 'وذكر أرسطاطاليس' to the end. Notice the ten items and their examples.
The categories are: substance, place, quality, time, quantity, relation, possession, posture, action, passion.
2
Match each category with its example
For each category, note the Arabic term and the example given. For instance, 'al-ʿayn' is substance, exemplified by 'human'.
Some examples are clear, like 'how' for quality, 'when' for time.
3
Understand the claim of exhaustiveness
The text ends by stating that no one can mention anything outside this total. This shows the philosophers' confidence in the completeness of the categories.
This claim will be examined in later lessons.
